Title: Human evolution

URL Source: https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution

Published Time: 2001-12-27T22:27:20Z

Markdown Content:
"Anthropogeny" redirects here. For the study of humans, see [Anthropology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anthropology "Anthropology").

[![Image 1](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/49/Ape_skeletons.png/500px-Ape_skeletons.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Ape_skeletons.png)

The [hominoids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hominoid "Hominoid") are descendants of a [common ancestor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Common_ancestor "Common ancestor").

_[Homo sapiens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homo\_sapiens "Homo sapiens")_ is a distinct species of the [hominid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hominid "Hominid") family of [primates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primates "Primates"), which also includes all the [great apes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_ape "Great ape").[[1]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-1) Over their [evolutionary](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolution "Evolution") history, humans gradually developed traits such as [bipedalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_skeletal_changes_due_to_bipedalism "Human skeletal changes due to bipedalism"), [dexterity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dexterity "Dexterity"), and [complex language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Complex_language "Complex language"),[[2]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-HallHallgr%C3%ADmsson2011c-2) as well as interbreeding with other [hominins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hominins "Hominins") (a tribe of the [African hominid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homininae "Homininae") subfamily),[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-:3-3) indicating that human evolution was not linear but weblike.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Ackermann_2015-4)[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Denisovans_&_Neandertals-5)[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Human_Hybrids-6)[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Mosaic_humans,_the_hybrid_species-7) The study of the origins of humans involves [several](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interdisciplinary "Interdisciplinary") scientific disciplines, including [physical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Biological_anthropology "Biological anthropology") and [evolutionary anthropology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolutionary_anthropology "Evolutionary anthropology"), [paleontology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paleontology "Paleontology"), and [genetics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetics "Genetics"); the field is also known by the terms **anthropogeny**, **anthropogenesis**, and **anthropogony**[[8]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-8)[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Marlowe_54%E2%80%9367-9)—with the latter two sometimes used to refer to the related subject of [hominization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hominization "Hominization").

[Primates diverged](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Primate_evolution "Primate evolution") from other [mammals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mammal "Mammal") about [85](https://geoltime.github.io/?Ma=85)million years ago ([mya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Myr "Myr")), in the [Late Cretaceous](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Late_Cretaceous "Late Cretaceous") period, with their earliest fossils appearing over 55 mya, during the [Paleocene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paleocene "Paleocene").[[10]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-10) Primates produced successive clades leading to the [ape](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ape "Ape") superfamily, which gave rise to the hominid and the [gibbon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gibbon "Gibbon") families; these diverged some 15–20 mya. African and [Asian hominids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ponginae "Ponginae") (including [orangutans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orangutan "Orangutan")) diverged about 14 mya. [Hominins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hominini "Hominini") (including the [Australopithecine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecine "Australopithecine") and [Panina](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Panina "Panina") subtribes) parted from the [Gorillini](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gorillini "Gorillini") tribe between 8 and 9 mya; Australopithecine (including the extinct biped ancestors of humans) separated from the _Pan_ genus (containing [chimpanzees](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chimpanzee "Chimpanzee") and [bonobos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bonobo "Bonobo")) 4–7 mya.[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-:4-11) The _[Homo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homo "Homo")_ genus is evidenced by the appearance of _[H.habilis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H.\_habilis "H. habilis")_ over 2 mya,[[a]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-habilis-12) while [anatomically modern humans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatomically_modern_human "Anatomically modern human") emerged in Africa approximately 300,000 years ago.

### Early evolution of primates

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&action=edit&section=2 "Edit section: Early evolution of primates")]

The evolutionary history of primates can be traced back 65 million years.[[12]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMaxwell1984296-13)[[13]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-14)[[14]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-15)[[15]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMartin200112032%E2%80%9312038-16)[[16]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-17) The details of the origins and early evolution of primates are however still unknwn to a large extant due to scarcity of fossil evidence. One of the oldest known primate-like mammal species, the _[Plesiadapis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Plesiadapis "Plesiadapis")_, came from North America;[[17]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-18)[[18]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-19)[[19]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-20)[[20]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-21)[[21]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-22)[[22]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-23) another, _[Archicebus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archicebus "Archicebus")_, came from [China](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/China "China").[[23]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-NYT-20130605-24) Other similar basal primates were widespread in Eurasia and Africa during the tropical conditions of the Paleocene and [Eocene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eocene "Eocene").

[![Image 2](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Notharctus_tenebrosus_AMNH.jpg/250px-Notharctus_tenebrosus_AMNH.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Notharctus_tenebrosus_AMNH.jpg)

_[Notharctus tenebrosus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Notharctus\_tenebrosus "Notharctus tenebrosus")_, [American Museum of Natural History](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/American_Museum_of_Natural_History "American Museum of Natural History"), New York

David R. Begun[[24]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Kordos-p17-25) theorized that early primates flourished in Eurasia and Africa. The surviving tropical population of primates—which is seen most completely in the Upper Eocene and lowermost [Oligocene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oligocene "Oligocene") fossil beds of the [Faiyum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Faiyum "Faiyum") depression southwest of [Cairo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cairo "Cairo")—gave rise to all extant primate species, including the [lemurs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lemur "Lemur") of [Madagascar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Madagascar "Madagascar"), [lorises](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Loris "Loris") of Southeast Asia, [galagos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galago "Galago") or "bush babies" of Africa, and to the [anthropoids](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Simian "Simian"), which are the [Platyrrhines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/New_World_monkey "New World monkey") or New World monkeys, the [Catarrhines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catarrhines "Catarrhines") or Old World monkeys, and the great apes, including humans and other hominids.

The earliest known catarrhine is _[Kamoyapithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kamoyapithecus "Kamoyapithecus")_ from the uppermost Oligocene at Eragaleit in the northern [Great Rift Valley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Great_Rift_Valley,_Kenya "Great Rift Valley, Kenya") in Kenya, dated to 24 million years ago.[[25]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTECameron2004[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidfnqzb4_UVfkCpgPA76_76]-26) Its ancestry is thought to be species related to _[Aegyptopithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aegyptopithecus "Aegyptopithecus")_, _[Propliopithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Propliopithecus "Propliopithecus")_, and _[Parapithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Parapithecus "Parapithecus")_ from the Faiyum, at around 35 mya.[[26]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWallace2004[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidYoyQQEOKGVQCpgPA240_240]-27) In 2010, _[Saadanius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Saadanius "Saadanius")_ was described as a close relative of the last common ancestor of the [crown](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Crown_group "Crown group") catarrhines, and tentatively dated to 29–28 mya, helping to fill an 11-million-year gap in the fossil record.[[27]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-2010Zalmout-28)

[![Image 3](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fb/Proconsul_skeleton_reconstitution_%28University_of_Zurich%29.JPG/250px-Proconsul_skeleton_reconstitution_%28University_of_Zurich%29.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Proconsul_skeleton_reconstitution_(University_of_Zurich).JPG)

Reconstructed tailless _[Proconsul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proconsul\_(mammal) "Proconsul (mammal)")_ skeleton

In the [Early Miocene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Early_Miocene "Early Miocene"), about 22 million years ago, the many kinds of [arboreally](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arboreal_locomotion "Arboreal locomotion")-adapted (tree-dwelling) primitive catarrhines from East Africa suggest a long history of prior diversification. [Fossils](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fossil "Fossil") at 20 million years ago include fragments attributed to _[Victoriapithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Victoriapithecus "Victoriapithecus")_, the earliest Old World monkey. Among the genera thought to be in the [ape](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ape "Ape") lineage leading up to 13 million years ago are _[Proconsul](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proconsul\_(mammal) "Proconsul (mammal)")_, _[Rangwapithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rangwapithecus "Rangwapithecus")_, _[Dendropithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dendropithecus "Dendropithecus")_, _[Limnopithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Limnopithecus&action=edit&redlink=1 "Limnopithecus (page does not exist)")_, _[Nacholapithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nacholapithecus "Nacholapithecus")_, _[Equatorius](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Equatorius "Equatorius")_, _[Nyanzapithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nyanzapithecus\_pickfordi "Nyanzapithecus pickfordi")_, _[Afropithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afropithecus "Afropithecus")_, _Heliopithecus_, and _[Kenyapithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kenyapithecus "Kenyapithecus")_, all from East Africa.

The presence of other generalized non-cercopithecids of [Middle Miocene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_Miocene "Middle Miocene") from sites far distant, such as _[Otavipithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Otavipithecus "Otavipithecus")_ from cave deposits in Namibia, and _[Pierolapithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pierolapithecus "Pierolapithecus")_ and _[Dryopithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dryopithecus "Dryopithecus")_ from France, Spain and Austria, is evidence of a wide diversity of forms across Africa and the Mediterranean basin during the relatively warm and equable climatic regimes of the Early and Middle Miocene. The youngest of the [Miocene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miocene "Miocene") hominoids, _[Oreopithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oreopithecus "Oreopithecus")_, is from coal beds in Italy that have been dated to 9 million years ago.

Molecular evidence indicates that the lineage of gibbons diverged from the line of great apes some 18–12 mya, and that of orangutans (subfamily [Ponginae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ponginae "Ponginae"))[[b]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-29) diverged from the other great apes at about 12 million years; there are no fossils that clearly document the ancestry of gibbons, which may have originated in a so-far-unknown Southeast Asian hominoid population, but fossil proto-orangutans may be represented by _[Sivapithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sivapithecus "Sivapithecus")_ from India and _[Griphopithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Griphopithecus "Griphopithecus")_ from Turkey, dated to around 10 mya.[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTESrivastava2009[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidkCerOsM8XMwCpgPA87_87]-30)

Hominidae subfamily [Homininae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homininae "Homininae") (African hominids) diverged from Ponginae (orangutans) about 14 mya. Hominins (including humans and the Australopithecine and [Panina](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Panina "Panina") subtribes) parted from the [Gorillini](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gorillini "Gorillini") tribe (gorillas) between 8 and 9 mya; Australopithecine (including the extinct biped ancestors of humans) separated from the _Pan_ genus (containing chimpanzees and bonobos) 4–7 mya.[[11]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-:4-11) The _Homo_ genus is evidenced by the appearance of _H.habilis_ over 2 mya,[[a]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-habilis-12) while anatomically modern humans emerged in Africa approximately 300,000 years ago.

### Divergence of the human clade from other great apes

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&action=edit&section=3 "Edit section: Divergence of the human clade from other great apes")]

[![Image 4](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/016_Alpha_male_chimpanzee_walking_at_Kibale_forest_National_Park_Photo_by_Giles_Laurent.jpg/250px-016_Alpha_male_chimpanzee_walking_at_Kibale_forest_National_Park_Photo_by_Giles_Laurent.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:016_Alpha_male_chimpanzee_walking_at_Kibale_forest_National_Park_Photo_by_Giles_Laurent.jpg)

Genetic data suggest that [chimpanzees](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chimpanzee "Chimpanzee") and humans may have diverged from a common ancestor between 9 and 7 million years ago.[[29]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-31)

Species close to the last common ancestor of gorillas, chimpanzees and humans may be represented by _[Nakalipithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nakalipithecus "Nakalipithecus")_ fossils found in Kenya and _[Ouranopithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ouranopithecus "Ouranopithecus")_ found in Greece. Molecular evidence suggests that between 8 and 4 million years ago, first the gorillas, and then the chimpanzees (genus _Pan_) split off from the line leading to the humans. Human DNA is approximately 98.4% identical to that of chimpanzees when comparing single nucleotide polymorphisms (see [human evolutionary genetics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolutionary_genetics "Human evolutionary genetics")). The fossil record, however, of gorillas and chimpanzees is limited; both poor preservation – rain forest soils tend to be acidic and dissolve bone – and [sampling bias](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sampling_bias "Sampling bias") probably contribute to this problem.

Other hominins probably adapted to the drier environments outside the equatorial belt; and there they encountered antelope, hyenas, dogs, pigs, elephants, horses, and others. The equatorial belt contracted after about 8 million years ago, and there is very little fossil evidence for the split—thought to have occurred around that time—of the hominin lineage from the lineages of gorillas and chimpanzees. The earliest fossils argued by some to belong to the human lineage are _Sahelanthropus tchadensis_ (7 Ma) and _Orrorin tugenensis_ (6 Ma), followed by _Ardipithecus_ (5.5–4.4 Ma), with species _Ar. kadabba_ and _[Ar. ramidus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ar.\_ramidus "Ar. ramidus")_.

It has been argued in a study of the life history of _Ar. ramidus_ that the species provides evidence for a suite of anatomical and behavioral adaptations in very early hominins unlike any species of extant great ape.[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-aramidus-32) This study demonstrated affinities between the skull morphology of _Ar. ramidus_ and that of infant and juvenile chimpanzees, suggesting the species evolved a juvenalised or [paedomorphic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paedomorphic "Paedomorphic") craniofacial morphology via [heterochronic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heterochrony "Heterochrony") dissociation of growth trajectories. It was also argued that the species provides support for the notion that very early hominins, akin to bonobos (_Pan paniscus_) the less aggressive species of the genus _Pan_, may have evolved via the process of [self-domestication](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Self-domestication "Self-domestication"). Consequently, arguing against the so-called "chimpanzee referential model"[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-sayers-33) the authors suggest it is no longer tenable to use chimpanzee (_Pan troglodytes_) social and mating behaviors in models of early hominin social evolution. When commenting on the absence of aggressive canine morphology in _Ar. ramidus_ and the implications this has for the evolution of hominin social psychology, they wrote:

> Of course _Ar. ramidus_ differs significantly from bonobos, bonobos having retained a functional canine honing complex. However, the fact that _Ar. ramidus_ shares with bonobos reduced sexual dimorphism, and a more paedomorphic form relative to chimpanzees, suggests that the developmental and social adaptations evident in bonobos may be of assistance in future reconstructions of early hominin social and sexual psychology. In fact the trend towards increased maternal care, female mate selection and self-domestication may have been stronger and more refined in _Ar. ramidus_ than what we see in bonobos.[[30]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-aramidus-32): 128

The authors argue that many of the basic human adaptations evolved in the ancient forest and woodland ecosystems of late [Miocene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Miocene "Miocene") and early [Pliocene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pliocene "Pliocene") Africa. Consequently, they argue that humans may not represent evolution from a chimpanzee-like ancestor as has traditionally been supposed. This suggests many modern human adaptations represent [phylogenetically](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phylogeny "Phylogeny") deep traits and that the behavior and morphology of chimpanzees may have evolved subsequent to the split with the common ancestor they share with humans.

### Genus _Australopithecus_

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&action=edit&section=4 "Edit section: Genus Australopithecus")]

[![Image 5](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ac/Bienvenida_al_Museo_de_Lucy.jpg/250px-Bienvenida_al_Museo_de_Lucy.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Bienvenida_al_Museo_de_Lucy.jpg)

Reconstruction of "[Lucy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lucy_(Australopithecus) "Lucy (Australopithecus)")"

The genus _[Australopithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus "Australopithecus")_ evolved in eastern Africa around 4 million years ago before spreading throughout the continent and eventually becoming extinct 2 million years ago. During this time period various forms of australopiths existed, including _[Australopithecus anamensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_anamensis "Australopithecus anamensis")_, _[A. afarensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_afarensis "Australopithecus afarensis")_, _[A. sediba](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_sediba "Australopithecus sediba")_, and _[A. africanus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_africanus "Australopithecus africanus")_. There is still some debate among academics whether certain African hominid species of this time, such as _[A. robustus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_robustus "Australopithecus robustus")_ and _[A. boisei](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_boisei "Australopithecus boisei")_, constitute members of the same genus; if so, they would be considered to be "robust australopiths" while the others would be considered "gracile australopiths". However, if these species do indeed constitute their own genus, then they may be given their own name, _Paranthropus_.

*   _[Australopithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus "Australopithecus")_ (4–1.8 Ma), with species _[A. anamensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_anamensis "Australopithecus anamensis")_, _[A. afarensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_afarensis "Australopithecus afarensis")_, _[A. africanus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_africanus "Australopithecus africanus")_, _[A. bahrelghazali](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_bahrelghazali "Australopithecus bahrelghazali")_, _[A. garhi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_garhi "Australopithecus garhi")_, and _[A. sediba](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_sediba "Australopithecus sediba")_;
*   _[Kenyanthropus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kenyanthropus "Kenyanthropus")_ (3–2.7 Ma), with species _[K. platyops](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kenyanthropus "Kenyanthropus")_;
*   _[Paranthropus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paranthropus "Paranthropus")_ (3–1.2 Ma), with species _[P. aethiopicus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paranthropus\_aethiopicus "Paranthropus aethiopicus")_, _[P. boisei](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paranthropus\_boisei "Paranthropus boisei")_, and _[P. robustus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paranthropus\_robustus "Paranthropus robustus")_

A new proposed species _[Australopithecus deyiremeda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_deyiremeda "Australopithecus deyiremeda")_ is claimed to have been discovered living at the same time period of _[A. afarensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_afarensis "Australopithecus afarensis")_. There is debate whether _[A. deyiremeda](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_deyiremeda "Australopithecus deyiremeda")_ is a new species or is _[A. afarensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_afarensis "Australopithecus afarensis")_.[[32]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-34)_Australopithecus prometheus_, otherwise known as [Little Foot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Little_Foot "Little Foot"), has recently been dated at 3.67 million years old through a new dating technique, making the genus _Australopithecus_ as old as _afarensis_.[[33]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-35) Given the opposable big toe found on Little Foot, it seems that the specimen was a good climber. It is thought given the night predators of the region that he built a nesting platform at night in the trees in a similar fashion to chimpanzees and gorillas.

[![Image 6](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Chimp%27s_Nest.jpg/250px-Chimp%27s_Nest.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Chimp%27s_Nest.jpg)

Chimpanzee nest. Later [hominins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hominin "Hominin") may have developed [niche creating](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Niche_construction "Niche construction") shelter-building traditions from such earlier [nest-building practices](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nest-building_in_primates "Nest-building in primates").

Evolution of genus _Homo_
-------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&action=edit&section=5 "Edit section: Evolution of genus Homo")]

Main article: [Homo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homo "Homo")

The earliest documented representative of the genus _Homo_ is _[Homo habilis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homo\_habilis "Homo habilis")_, which evolved around [2.8](https://geoltime.github.io/?Ma=2.8)million years ago,[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-autogenerated1-36) and is arguably the earliest species for which there is positive evidence of the use of stone tools. The brains of these early hominins were about the same size as that of a chimpanzee, although it has been suggested that this was the time in which the human [SRGAP2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SRGAP2 "SRGAP2")[gene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene "Gene") doubled, producing a more rapid wiring of the frontal cortex. During the next million years a process of rapid [encephalization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Encephalization "Encephalization") occurred, and with the arrival of _[Homo erectus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homo\_erectus "Homo erectus")_ and _[Homo ergaster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homo\_ergaster "Homo ergaster")_ in the [fossil record](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fossil#Dating "Fossil"), cranial capacity had doubled to 850 cm 3.[[35]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTESwisherCurtisLewin2001-37) (Such an increase in human brain size is equivalent to each generation having 125,000 more [neurons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neuron "Neuron") than their parents.) It is believed that _H.erectus_ and _H.ergaster_ were the first to use fire and complex tools, and were the first of the hominin line to leave Africa, spreading throughout Africa, Asia, and Europe between [1.3 to 1.8](https://geoltime.github.io/?Ma=1.3%E2%80%931.8) million years ago.

According to the recent African origin theory, modern humans evolved in Africa possibly from _[H.heidelbergensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H.\_heidelbergensis "H. heidelbergensis")_, _[H.rhodesiensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H.\_rhodesiensis "H. rhodesiensis")_ or _[H.antecessor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H.\_antecessor "H. antecessor")_ and migrated out of the continent some 50,000 to 100,000 years ago, gradually replacing local populations of _H.erectus_, [Denisova hominins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Denisova_hominin "Denisova hominin"), _[H.floresiensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H.\_floresiensis "H. floresiensis")_, _[H.luzonensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H.\_luzonensis "H. luzonensis")_ and _[H.neanderthalensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H.\_neanderthalensis "H. neanderthalensis")_, whose ancestors had left Africa in earlier migrations.[[36]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStringer1994242-38)[[37]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcHenry2009265-39)[[38]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-40)[[39]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-41)[[40]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-42)[Archaic _Homo sapiens_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaic_humans "Archaic humans"), the forerunner of [anatomically modern humans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans "Anatomically modern humans"), evolved in the [Middle Paleolithic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic "Middle Paleolithic") between 400,000 and 250,000 years ago.[[41]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-43)[[42]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-44)[[43]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-45) Recent [DNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA "DNA") evidence suggests that several [haplotypes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haplotype "Haplotype") of [Neanderthal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neanderthal "Neanderthal") origin are present among all non-African populations, and Neanderthals and other hominins, such as Denisovans, may have contributed up to 6% of their [genome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genome "Genome") to present-day humans, suggestive of a [limited interbreeding between these species](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interbreeding_between_archaic_and_modern_humans "Interbreeding between archaic and modern humans").[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-pmid21179161-46)[[45]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-pmid20439435-47)[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-10.1126/science.1209202-48) According to some anthropologists, the transition to [behavioral modernity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Behavioral_modernity "Behavioral modernity") with the development of symbolic culture, language, and specialized [lithic technology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lithic_technology "Lithic technology") happened around 50,000 years ago (beginning of the [Upper Paleolithic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic "Upper Paleolithic")),[[47]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-49) although others point to evidence of a gradual change over a longer time span during the Middle Paleolithic.[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Mcbrearty_Brooks-50)

[![Image 7](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/Homo-Stammbaum%2C_Version_Stringer-en.svg/500px-Homo-Stammbaum%2C_Version_Stringer-en.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Homo-Stammbaum,_Version_Stringer-en.svg)

A model of the phylogeny of _H. sapiens_ during the [Middle Paleolithic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic "Middle Paleolithic"). The horizontal axis represents geographic location; the vertical axis represents time in [millions of years ago](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Year#Abbreviations_yr_and_ya "Year") (Mya).[[49]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-51)_Homo Erectus_ is shown spreading across Eurasia starting around 1.8 Mya. _Homo heidelbergensis_ is shown diverging into Neanderthals, Denisovans and _H.sapiens_. With the expansion of _H.sapiens_ after 0.2 Mya, Neanderthals, Denisovans and unspecified archaic African hominins are shown as [again subsumed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Interbreeding_between_archaic_and_modern_humans "Interbreeding between archaic and modern humans") into the _H.sapiens_ lineage. Admixture events in modern African populations are also indicated.

_Homo sapiens_ is the only [extant species](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extant_species "Extant species") of its genus, _Homo_. While some (extinct) _Homo_ species might have been ancestors of _Homo sapiens_, many, perhaps most, were likely "cousins", having [speciated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speciate "Speciate") away from the ancestral hominin line.[[50]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-52)[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBryson2004522%E2%80%93543-53) There is yet no consensus as to which of these groups should be considered a separate species and which should be subspecies; this may be due to the dearth of fossils or to the slight differences used to classify species in the genus _Homo_.[[51]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBryson2004522%E2%80%93543-53) The [Sahara pump theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sahara_pump_theory "Sahara pump theory") (describing an occasionally passable ["wet" Sahara](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wet_Sahara "Wet Sahara") desert) provides one possible explanation of the intermittent migration and speciation in the genus _Homo_.

Based on archaeological and paleontological evidence, it has been possible to infer, to some extent, the ancient dietary practices[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-NYT-20150813-54) of various _Homo_ species and to study the role of diet in physical and behavioral evolution within _Homo_.[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Leonard_2007-55)[[54]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWalker2007[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid6mxZ1hNBHgkCpgPA3_3%E2%80%9310]-56)[[55]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEUngarTeaford2002-57)[[56]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBogin1997[httpswebarchiveorgweb20031203003838httpcitdscarutorontocaANTAO1ProjectsBoginhtml_96%E2%80%93142]-58)[[57]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-59)

Some anthropologists and archaeologists subscribe to the [Toba catastrophe theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toba_catastrophe_theory "Toba catastrophe theory"), which posits that the [supereruption](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Supereruption "Supereruption") of [Lake Toba](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lake_Toba "Lake Toba") on Sumatra in Indonesia some 70,000 years ago caused global starvation,[[58]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-60) killing the majority of humans and creating a [population bottleneck](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Population_bottleneck "Population bottleneck") that affected the genetic inheritance of all humans today.[[59]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-61) The genetic and archaeological evidence for this remains in question however.[[60]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-62) A 2023 genetic study suggests that a similar human [population bottleneck](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Population_bottleneck "Population bottleneck") of between 1,000 and 100,000 survivors occurred "around 930,000 and 813,000 years ago ... lasted for about 117,000 years and brought human ancestors close to extinction."[[61]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-NYT-20230831-63)[[62]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-SCI-20230831-64)

### _H. habilis_ and _H. gautengensis_

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&action=edit&section=6 "Edit section: H. habilis and H. gautengensis")]

_Homo habilis_ lived from about 2.8[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-autogenerated1-36) to 1.4 Ma. The species evolved in South and East Africa in the [Late Pliocene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Piacenzian "Piacenzian") or [Early Pleistocene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Early_Pleistocene "Early Pleistocene"), 2.5–2 Ma, when it diverged from the australopithecines with the development of smaller molars and larger brains. One of the first known hominins, it made [tools from stone](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stone_tool "Stone tool") and perhaps animal bones, leading to its name _homo_ _habilis_ (Latin 'handy man') bestowed by discoverer [Louis Leakey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louis_Leakey "Louis Leakey"). Some scientists have proposed moving this species from _Homo_ into _Australopithecus_ due to the morphology of its skeleton being more adapted to [living in trees](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arboreal_locomotion "Arboreal locomotion") rather than [walking on two legs](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bipedalism "Bipedalism") like later hominins.[[63]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-65)

In May 2010, a new species, _[Homo gautengensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homo\_gautengensis "Homo gautengensis")_, was discovered in South Africa.[[64]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-toothy-66)

### _H. rudolfensis_ and _H. georgicus_

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&action=edit&section=7 "Edit section: H. rudolfensis and H. georgicus")]

These are proposed species names for fossils from about 1.9–1.6 Ma, whose relation to _Homo habilis_ is not yet clear.

*   _Homo rudolfensis_ refers to a single, incomplete skull from Kenya. Scientists have suggested that this was a specimen of _Homo habilis_, but this has not been confirmed.[[65]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-67)
*   _[Homo georgicus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homo\_georgicus "Homo georgicus")_, from [Georgia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georgia_(country) "Georgia (country)"), may be an intermediate form between _Homo habilis_ and _Homo erectus_,[[66]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-68) or a subspecies of _Homo erectus_.[[67]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-69)

### _H. ergaster_ and _H. erectus_

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&action=edit&section=8 "Edit section: H. ergaster and H. erectus")]

[![Image 8](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/31/Homo-erectus_Turkana-Boy_%28Ausschnitt%29_Fundort_Nariokotome%2C_Kenia%2C_Rekonstruktion_im_Neanderthal_Museum.jpg/250px-Homo-erectus_Turkana-Boy_%28Ausschnitt%29_Fundort_Nariokotome%2C_Kenia%2C_Rekonstruktion_im_Neanderthal_Museum.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Homo-erectus_Turkana-Boy_(Ausschnitt)_Fundort_Nariokotome,_Kenia,_Rekonstruktion_im_Neanderthal_Museum.jpg)

Reconstruction of [Turkana Boy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turkana_Boy "Turkana Boy") who lived 1.5 to 1.6 million years ago

The first fossils of _Homo erectus_ were discovered by Dutch physician [Eugene Dubois](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eugene_Dubois "Eugene Dubois") in 1891 on the Indonesian island of Java. He originally named the material _[Anthropopithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anthropopithecus "Anthropopithecus") erectus_ (1892–1893, considered at this point as a chimpanzee-like fossil primate) and _[Pithecanthropus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pithecanthropus "Pithecanthropus") erectus_ (1893–1894, changing his mind as of based on its morphology, which he considered to be intermediate between that of humans and apes).[[68]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-70) Years later, in the 20th century, the German physician and [paleoanthropologist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paleoanthropologist "Paleoanthropologist")[Franz Weidenreich](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Franz_Weidenreich "Franz Weidenreich") (1873–1948) compared in detail the characters of Dubois' [Java Man](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Java_Man "Java Man"), then named _Pithecanthropus erectus_, with the characters of the [Peking Man](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Peking_Man "Peking Man"), then named _Sinanthropus pekinensis_. Weidenreich concluded in 1940 that because of their anatomical similarity with modern humans it was necessary to gather all these specimens of Java and China in a single species of the genus _Homo_, the species _H.erectus_.[[69]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-71)[[70]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-72)

_Homo erectus_ lived from about 1.8 Ma to about 108,000 years ago.[[71]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Rizal2020-73) This population appears to have died out when the savannah corridors closed, and tropical jungle took over.;[[72]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-74) however, nearby _[H.floresiensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H.\_floresiensis "H. floresiensis")_ survived it. The early phase of _H.erectus_, from 1.8 to 1.25 Ma, is considered by some to be a separate species, _H.ergaster_, or as _H.erectus ergaster_, a subspecies of _H.erectus_. Many paleoanthropologists now use the term _Homo ergaster_ for the non-Asian forms of this group, and reserve _H.erectus_ only for those fossils that are found in Asia and meet certain skeletal and dental requirements which differ slightly from _H.ergaster_.

In Africa in the Early Pleistocene, 1.5–1 Ma, some populations of _Homo habilis_ are thought to have evolved larger brains and to have made more elaborate stone tools; these differences and others are sufficient for anthropologists to classify them as a new species, _Homo erectus_—in Africa.[[73]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-75) This species also may have used fire to cook meat. [Richard Wrangham](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catching_Fire:_How_Cooking_Made_Us_Human "Catching Fire: How Cooking Made Us Human") notes that _Homo_ seems to have been ground dwelling, with reduced intestinal length, smaller dentition, and "brains [swollen] to their current, horrendously fuel-inefficient size",[[74]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-76) and hypothesizes that control of fire and cooking, which released increased nutritional value, was the key adaptation that separated _Homo_ from tree-sleeping Australopithecines.[[75]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-77)

### _H. cepranensis_ and _H. antecessor_

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&action=edit&section=9 "Edit section: H. cepranensis and H. antecessor")]

These are proposed as species intermediate between _H.erectus_ and _H.heidelbergensis_.

*   _H. antecessor_ is known from fossils from Spain and England that are dated 1.2 Ma–500 [ka](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Year#SI_prefix_multipliers "Year").[[76]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-78)[[77]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-79)
*   _[H. cepranensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homo\_cepranensis "Homo cepranensis")_ refers to a single skull cap from Italy, estimated to be about 800,000 years old.[[78]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-80)

_H. heidelbergensis_ ("Heidelberg Man") lived from about 800,000 to about 300,000 years ago. It is also proposed as _Homo sapiens heidelbergensis_ or _Homo sapiens paleohungaricus_.[[79]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-81)

### _H. rhodesiensis_, and the Gawis cranium

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&action=edit&section=11 "Edit section: H. rhodesiensis, and the Gawis cranium")]

*   _H. rhodesiensis_, estimated to be 300,000–125,000 years old. Most current researchers place Rhodesian Man within the group of _Homo heidelbergensis_, though other designations such as archaic _Homo sapiens_ and _Homo sapiens rhodesiensis_ have been proposed.
*   In February 2006 a fossil, the [Gawis cranium](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gawis_cranium "Gawis cranium"), was found which might possibly be a species intermediate between _H.erectus_ and _H.sapiens_ or one of many evolutionary dead ends. The skull from Gawis, Ethiopia, is believed to be 500,000–250,000 years old. Only summary details are known, and the finders have not yet released a peer-reviewed study. Gawis man's facial features suggest that it is either an intermediate species or an example of a "Bodo man" female.[[80]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-82)

### Neanderthal and Denisovan

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&action=edit&section=12 "Edit section: Neanderthal and Denisovan")]

[![Image 9](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Homo_sapiens_neanderthalensis-Mr._N.jpg/250px-Homo_sapiens_neanderthalensis-Mr._N.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Homo_sapiens_neanderthalensis-Mr._N.jpg)

Reconstruction of an elderly Neanderthal man

_Homo neanderthalensis_, alternatively designated as _Homo sapiens neanderthalensis_,[[81]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-83) lived in Europe and Asia from 400,000[[82]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-84) to about 28,000 years ago.[[83]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-85) There are a number of clear anatomical differences between [anatomically modern humans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans "Anatomically modern humans") (AMH) and Neanderthal specimens, many relating to the superior Neanderthal adaptation to cold environments. Neanderthal [surface to volume ratio](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Surface_to_volume_ratio "Surface to volume ratio") was even lower than that among modern [Inuit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Inuit "Inuit") populations, indicating superior retention of body heat.

Neanderthals also had significantly larger brains, as shown from brain endocasts, casting doubt on their intellectual inferiority to modern humans. However, the higher body mass of Neanderthals may have required larger brain mass for body control.[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-dunbar-86) Also, recent research by Pearce, [Stringer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chris_Stringer "Chris Stringer"), and Dunbar has shown important differences in brain architecture. The larger size of the Neanderthal orbital chamber and [occipital lobe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Occipital_lobe "Occipital lobe") suggests that they had a better visual acuity than modern humans, useful in the dimmer light of glacial Europe.

Neanderthals may have had less [brain capacity available for social functions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dunbar%27s_number "Dunbar's number"). Inferring social group size from endocranial volume (minus occipital lobe size) suggests that Neanderthal groups may have been limited to 120 individuals, compared to 144[_[citation needed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation\_needed "Wikipedia:Citation needed")_][[85]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-87) possible relationships for modern humans. Larger social groups could imply that modern humans had less risk of inbreeding within their clan, trade over larger areas (confirmed in the distribution of stone tools), and faster spread of social and technological innovations. All these may have all contributed to modern _Homo sapiens_ replacing Neanderthal populations by 28,000 BP.[[84]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-dunbar-86)

Earlier evidence from sequencing mitochondrial DNA suggested that no significant gene flow occurred between _H. neanderthalensis_ and _H. sapiens_, and that the two were separate species that shared a common ancestor about 660,000 years ago.[[86]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-88)[[87]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-89)[[88]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-90) However, a sequencing of the Neanderthal genome in 2010 indicated that Neanderthals did indeed interbreed with anatomically modern humans c. 45,000-80,000 years ago, around the time modern humans migrated out from Africa, but before they dispersed throughout Europe, Asia and elsewhere.[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-neandersequence-91) The genetic sequencing of a 40,000-year-old [human skeleton from Romania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pe%C8%99tera_cu_Oase "Peștera cu Oase") showed that 11% of its genome was Neanderthal, implying the individual had a Neanderthal ancestor 4–6 generations previously,[[90]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-92) in addition to a contribution from earlier interbreeding in the Middle East. Though this interbred Romanian population seems not to have been ancestral to modern humans, the finding indicates that interbreeding happened repeatedly.[[91]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-93)

All modern non-African humans have about 1% to 4% (or 1.5% to 2.6% by more recent data) of their DNA derived from Neanderthals.[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-greenetal-94)[[89]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-neandersequence-91)[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-A_high-coverage_Neandertal_genome_f-95) This finding is consistent with recent studies indicating that the divergence of some human alleles dates to one Ma, although this interpretation has been questioned.[[94]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-96)[[95]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-97) Neanderthals and AMH _Homo sapiens_ could have co-existed in Europe for as long as 10,000 years, during which AMH populations exploded, vastly outnumbering Neanderthals, possibly outcompeting them by sheer numbers.[[96]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-98)

In 2008, archaeologists working at the site of [Denisova Cave](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Denisova_Cave "Denisova Cave") in the [Altai Mountains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Altai_Mountains "Altai Mountains") of [Siberia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Siberia "Siberia") uncovered a small bone fragment from the fifth finger of a juvenile member of another human species, the Denisovans.[[97]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-99) Artifacts, including a bracelet, excavated in the cave at the same level were [carbon dated](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Radiocarbon_dating "Radiocarbon dating") to around 40,000 BP. As DNA had survived in the fossil fragment due to the cool climate of the Denisova Cave, both mtDNA and nuclear DNA were sequenced.[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-pmid21179161-46)[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-P%C3%A4%C3%A4bo_et_al.-100)

While the divergence point of the mtDNA was unexpectedly deep in time,[[99]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-The_Scientist-101) the full genomic sequence suggested the Denisovans belonged to the same lineage as Neanderthals, with the two diverging shortly after their line split from the lineage that gave rise to modern humans.[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-pmid21179161-46) Modern humans are known to have overlapped with Neanderthals in Europe and the Near East for possibly more than 40,000 years,[[100]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-102) and the discovery raises the possibility that Neanderthals, Denisovans, and modern humans may have co-existed and interbred. The existence of this distant branch creates a much more complex picture of humankind during the [Late Pleistocene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Late_Pleistocene "Late Pleistocene") than previously thought.[[98]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-P%C3%A4%C3%A4bo_et_al.-100)[[101]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-103) Evidence has also been found that as much as 6% of the DNA of some modern [Melanesians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Melanesians "Melanesians") derive from Denisovans, indicating limited interbreeding in Southeast Asia.[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Reich_2011-104)[[103]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-105)

Alleles thought to have originated in Neanderthals and Denisovans have been identified at several genetic loci in the genomes of modern humans outside Africa. [Human leukocyte antigen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_leukocyte_antigen "Human leukocyte antigen") (HLA) haplotypes from Denisovans and Neanderthal represent more than half the HLA alleles of modern Eurasians,[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-10.1126/science.1209202-48) indicating strong positive selection for these [introgressed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Introgressed "Introgressed") alleles. Corinne Simoneti at Vanderbilt University, in Nashville and her team have found from medical records of 28,000 people of European descent that the presence of Neanderthal DNA segments may be associated with a higher rate of depression.[[104]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-106)

The flow of genes from Neanderthal populations to modern humans was not all one way. Sergi Castellano of the Max Planck Institute for [Evolutionary Anthropology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolutionary_Anthropology "Evolutionary Anthropology") reported in 2016 that while Denisovan and Neanderthal genomes are more related to each other than they are to us, Siberian Neanderthal genomes show more similarity to modern human genes than do European Neanderthal populations. This suggests Neanderthal populations interbred with modern humans around 100,000 years ago, probably somewhere in the Near East.[[105]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-107)

Studies of a Neanderthal child at Gibraltar show from brain development and tooth eruption that Neanderthal children may have matured more rapidly than _Homo sapiens_.[[106]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-108)

[![Image 10](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1d/Homo_floresiensis_v_2-0.jpg/250px-Homo_floresiensis_v_2-0.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Homo_floresiensis_v_2-0.jpg)

A facial reconstruction of _Homo floresiensis_

_H. floresiensis_, which lived from approximately 190,000 to 50,000 years [before present](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Before_present "Before present") (BP), has been nicknamed the _[hobbit](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hobbit "Hobbit")_ for its small size, possibly a result of [insular dwarfism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Insular_dwarfism "Insular dwarfism").[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-:0-109)_H. floresiensis_ is intriguing both for its size and its age, being an example of a recent species of the genus _Homo_ that exhibits derived traits not shared with modern humans. In other words, _H. floresiensis_ shares a common ancestor with modern humans, but split from the modern human lineage and followed a distinct evolutionary path. The main find was a skeleton believed to be a woman of about 30 years of age. Found in 2003, it has been dated to approximately 18,000 years old. The living woman was estimated to be one meter in height, with a brain volume of just 380 cm 3 (considered small for a chimpanzee and less than a third of the _H.sapiens_ average of 1400 cm 3).[[107]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-:0-109)

However, there is an ongoing debate over whether _H.floresiensis_ is indeed a separate species.[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-:1-110) Some scientists hold that _H. floresiensis_ was a modern _H. sapiens_ with pathological dwarfism.[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Martin-111) This hypothesis is supported in part, because some modern humans who live on [Flores](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flores "Flores"), the Indonesian island where the skeleton was found, are [pygmies](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pygmy_peoples "Pygmy peoples"). This, coupled with pathological dwarfism, could have resulted in a significantly diminutive human. The other major attack on _H. floresiensis_ as a separate species is that it was found with tools only associated with _H. sapiens_.[[109]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Martin-111)

The hypothesis of pathological dwarfism, however, fails to explain additional [anatomical features](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homo_floresiensis#Anatomy "Homo floresiensis") that are unlike those of modern humans (diseased or not) but much like those of ancient members of our genus. Aside from cranial features, these features include the form of bones in the wrist, forearm, shoulder, knees, and feet. Additionally, this hypothesis fails to explain the find of multiple examples of individuals with these same characteristics, indicating they were common to a large population, and not limited to one individual.[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-:1-110)

In 2016, fossil teeth and a partial jaw from hominins assumed to be ancestral to _H.floresiensis_ were discovered[[110]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Callaway2016b-112) at [Mata Menge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mata_Menge "Mata Menge"), about 74 km (46 mi) from Liang Bua. They date to about 700,000 years ago[[111]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Brumm2016-113) and are noted by Australian archaeologist Gerrit van den Bergh for being even smaller than the later fossils.[[112]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-114)

A small number of specimens from the island of [Luzon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Luzon "Luzon"), dated 50,000 to 67,000 years ago, have recently been assigned by their discoverers, based on dental characteristics, to a novel human species, _H. luzonensis_.[[113]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-115)

[![Image 11](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5e/Homo_sapiens_sapiens_%28Fundort_Jebel_Irhoud_Marokko%29.jpg/250px-Homo_sapiens_sapiens_%28Fundort_Jebel_Irhoud_Marokko%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Homo_sapiens_sapiens_(Fundort_Jebel_Irhoud_Marokko).jpg)

Reconstruction of early _Homo sapiens_ from [Jebel Irhoud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jebel_Irhoud "Jebel Irhoud"), Morocco c. 315 000 years BP

_H. sapiens_ (the adjective _[sapiens](https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sapiens "wikt:sapiens")_ is Latin for "wise" or "intelligent") emerged in Africa around 300,000 years ago, likely derived from _[H.heidelbergensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H.\_heidelbergensis "H. heidelbergensis")_ or a related lineage.[[114]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Schlebusch2017-116)[[115]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Guardian-117) In September 2019, scientists reported the computerized determination, based on 260 [CT scans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/CT_scan "CT scan"), of a virtual [skull shape](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_skull "Human skull") of the last common human ancestor to [modern humans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_human "Modern human") (_H. sapiens_), representative of the earliest modern humans, and suggested that modern humans arose between 260,000 and 350,000 years ago through a merging of populations in [East](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_Africa "East Africa") and South Africa.[[116]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-NYT-20190910-118)[[117]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-NAT-20190910-119)

Between 400,000 years ago and the second interglacial period in the [Middle Pleistocene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_Pleistocene "Middle Pleistocene"), around 250,000 years ago, the trend in [intra-cranial volume expansion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brain_size#Cranial_capacity "Brain size") and the elaboration of stone tool technologies developed, providing evidence for a transition from _H.erectus_ to _H.sapiens_. The direct evidence suggests there was a migration of _H.erectus_[out of Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans "Recent African origin of modern humans"), then a further speciation of _H.sapiens_ from _H.erectus_ in Africa. A subsequent migration (both within and out of Africa) eventually replaced the earlier dispersed _H.erectus_. This migration and origin theory is usually referred to as the "recent single-origin hypothesis" or "out of Africa" theory. _H.sapiens_[interbred with archaic humans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans "Archaic human admixture with modern humans") both in Africa and in Eurasia, in Eurasia notably with Neanderthals and Denisovans.[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-pmid21179161-46)[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Reich_2011-104)

The [Toba catastrophe theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toba_catastrophe_theory "Toba catastrophe theory"), which postulates a [population bottleneck](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Population_bottleneck "Population bottleneck") for _H. sapiens_ about 70,000 years ago,[[118]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-ambrose1998-120) was controversial from its first proposal in the 1990s and by the 2010s had very little support.[[119]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-121) Distinctive [human genetic variability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_genetic_variability "Human genetic variability") has arisen as the result of the [founder effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Founder_effect "Founder effect"), by [archaic admixture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans "Archaic human admixture with modern humans") and by [recent evolutionary pressures](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recent_human_evolution "Recent human evolution").

Since _[Homo sapiens](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homo\_sapiens "Homo sapiens")_ separated from its [last common ancestor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chimpanzee%E2%80%93human_last_common_ancestor "Chimpanzee–human last common ancestor") shared with [chimpanzees](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chimpanzee "Chimpanzee"), human evolution is characterized by a number of [morphological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Morphology_(biology) "Morphology (biology)"), [developmental](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_development_(biology) "Human development (biology)"), [physiological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_physiology "Human physiology"), [behavioral](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_behavior "Human behavior"), and environmental changes.[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Marlowe_54%E2%80%9367-9) Environmental (cultural) evolution discovered much later during the [Pleistocene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pleistocene "Pleistocene") played a significant role in human evolution observed via human transitions between subsistence systems.[[120]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-122)[[9]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Marlowe_54%E2%80%9367-9) The most significant of these adaptations are bipedalism, increased brain size, lengthened [ontogeny](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ontogeny "Ontogeny") (gestation and infancy), and decreased [sexual dimorphism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism "Sexual dimorphism"). The relationship between these changes is the subject of ongoing debate.[[121]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBoydSilk2003[[Category:Wikipedia_articles_needing_page_number_citations_from_February_2015]]%3Csup_class=%22noprint_Inline-Template_%22_style=%22white-space:nowrap;%22%3E[%3Ci%3E[[Wikipedia:Citing_sources|%3Cspan_title=%22This_citation_requires_a_reference_to_the_specific_page_or_range_of_pages_in_which_the_material_appears.%20(February_2015)%22%3Epage%C2%A0needed%3C/span%3E]]%3C/i%3E]%3C/sup%3E-123) Other significant morphological changes included the evolution of a [power and precision grip](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thumb#Grips "Thumb"), a change first occurring in _[H.erectus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H.\_erectus "H. erectus")_.[[122]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruesSnow1965[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid9WemAAAAIAAJpgPA1_1%E2%80%9339]-124)

[![Image 12](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c5/Akha_cropped.png/250px-Akha_cropped.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Akha_cropped.png)

Bipedalism shown by a man and a woman

[Bipedalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bipedalism "Bipedalism") (walking on two legs) is the basic adaptation of the hominid and is considered the main cause behind a suite of skeletal changes shared by all bipedal hominids. The earliest hominin, of presumably primitive bipedalism, is considered to be either _[Sahelanthropus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sahelanthropus "Sahelanthropus")_[[123]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Brunet2002-125) or _[Orrorin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orrorin "Orrorin")_, both of which arose some 6 to 7 million years ago. The non-bipedal knuckle-walkers, the [gorillas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gorilla "Gorilla") and chimpanzees, diverged from the hominin line over a period covering the same time, so either _Sahelanthropus_ or _Orrorin_ may be our last shared ancestor. _[Ardipithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ardipithecus "Ardipithecus")_, a full biped, arose approximately 5.6 million years ago.[[124]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-126)

The early bipeds eventually evolved into the australopithecines and still later into the genus _[Homo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homo "Homo")_. There are several theories of the adaptation value of bipedalism. It is possible that bipedalism was favored because it freed the hands for reaching and carrying food, saved energy during locomotion,[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Kwang_Hyun_2015_929%E2%80%93934-127) enabled long-distance running and hunting, provided an enhanced field of vision, and helped avoid hyperthermia by reducing the surface area exposed to direct sun; features all advantageous for thriving in the new savanna and woodland environment created as a result of the East African Rift Valley uplift versus the previous closed forest habitat.[[125]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Kwang_Hyun_2015_929%E2%80%93934-127)[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeSalleTattersall2008[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidBf4Sitw7YaICpgPA146_146]-128)[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTECurry2008106%E2%80%93109-129) A 2007 study provides support for the hypothesis that bipedalism evolved because it used less energy than quadrupedal knuckle-walking.[[128]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-130)[[129]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-131) However, recent studies suggest that bipedality without the [ability to use fire](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Control_of_fire_by_early_humans "Control of fire by early humans") would not have allowed global dispersal.[[130]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-132) This change in gait saw a lengthening of the legs proportionately when compared to the length of the arms, which were shortened through the removal of the need for [brachiation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brachiation "Brachiation"). Another change is the shape of the big toe. Recent studies suggest that australopithecines still lived part of the time in trees as a result of maintaining a grasping big toe. This was progressively lost in habilines.

Anatomically, the evolution of bipedalism has been accompanied by a large number of skeletal changes, not just to the legs and pelvis, but also to the [vertebral column](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_vertebral_column "Human vertebral column"), feet and ankles, and skull.[[131]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEAielloDean1990-133) The [femur](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Femur "Femur") evolved into a slightly more angular position to move the center of gravity toward the geometric center of the body. The knee and ankle joints became increasingly robust to better support increased weight. To support the increased weight on each vertebra in the upright position, the human vertebral column became S-shaped and the [lumbar vertebrae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lumbar_vertebrae "Lumbar vertebrae") became shorter and wider. In the feet the big toe moved into alignment with the other toes to help in forward locomotion. The arms and forearms shortened relative to the legs making it easier to run. The [foramen magnum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foramen_magnum "Foramen magnum") migrated under the skull and more anterior.[[132]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKondo1985-134)

The most significant changes occurred in the pelvic region, where the long downward facing [iliac blade](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ilium_(bone) "Ilium (bone)") was shortened and widened as a requirement for keeping the center of gravity stable while walking;[[28]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTESrivastava2009[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidkCerOsM8XMwCpgPA87_87]-30) bipedal hominids have a shorter but broader, bowl-like pelvis due to this. A drawback is that the birth canal of bipedal apes is smaller than in knuckle-walking apes, though there has been a widening of it in comparison to that of australopithecine and modern humans, thus permitting the passage of newborns due to the increase in cranial size. This is limited to the upper portion, since further increase can hinder normal bipedal movement.[[133]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStrickberger2000[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidfeMItLo5gwgCpgPA476_475%E2%80%93476]-135)

The shortening of the pelvis and smaller birth canal evolved as a requirement for bipedalism and had significant effects on the process of human birth, which is much more difficult in modern humans than in other primates. During human birth, because of the variation in size of the pelvic region, the fetal head must be in a transverse position (compared to the mother) during entry into the birth canal and rotate about 90 degrees upon exit.[[134]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrevathan2011[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidPotlqpl-jxgCpgPA20_20]-136) The smaller birth canal became a limiting factor to brain size increases in early humans and prompted a shorter gestation period leading to the relative immaturity of human offspring, who are unable to walk much before 12 months and have greater [neoteny](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neoteny "Neoteny"), compared to other primates, who are mobile at a much earlier age.[[127]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTECurry2008106%E2%80%93109-129) The increased brain growth after birth and the increased dependency of children on mothers had a major effect upon the female reproductive cycle,[[135]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-137) and the more frequent appearance of [alloparenting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alloparenting "Alloparenting") in humans when compared with other hominids.[[136]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-138) Delayed human sexual maturity also led to the evolution of [menopause](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Menopause "Menopause") with one explanation, the [grandmother hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Grandmother_hypothesis "Grandmother hypothesis"), providing that elderly women could better pass on their genes by taking care of their daughter's offspring, as compared to having more children of their own.[[137]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-139)[[138]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-140)

[![Image 13](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Skull_evolution.png/960px-Skull_evolution.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Skull_evolution.png)

Skulls of successive (or near-successive, depending on the source) human evolutionary ancestors,[[c]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-143) up until 'modern' _Homo sapiens_

 * [Mya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mya_(unit) "Mya (unit)") – million years ago, [kya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kya_(unit) "Kya (unit)") – thousand years ago

[![Image 14](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Brain_size_and_tooth_size_in_hominins.jpg/500px-Brain_size_and_tooth_size_in_hominins.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Brain_size_and_tooth_size_in_hominins.jpg)

Brain size and tooth size in hominins

The human species eventually developed a much larger brain than that of other primates—typically 1,330 cm 3 (81 cu in) in modern humans, nearly three times the size of a chimpanzee or gorilla brain.[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Schoeneman-144) After a period of stasis with _Australopithecus anamensis_ and _Ardipithecus_, species which had smaller brains as a result of their bipedal locomotion,[[142]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-145) the pattern of [encephalization](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Encephalization "Encephalization") started with _Homo habilis_, whose 600 cm 3 (37 cu in) brain was slightly larger than that of chimpanzees. This evolution continued in _Homo erectus_ with 800–1,100 cm 3 (49–67 cu in), and reached a maximum in Neanderthals with 1,200–1,900 cm 3 (73–116 cu in), larger even than modern _Homo sapiens_. This brain increase manifested during postnatal [brain growth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neural_development "Neural development"), far exceeding that of other apes ([heterochrony](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heterochrony "Heterochrony")). It also allowed for extended periods of [social learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Observational_learning "Observational learning") and [language acquisition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Language_acquisition "Language acquisition") in juvenile humans, beginning as much as 2 million years ago. Encephalization may be due to a dependency on calorie-dense, difficult-to-acquire food.[[143]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-146)

Furthermore, the changes in the structure of [human brains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_brain "Human brain") may be even more significant than the increase in size.[[144]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Park2007-147)[[145]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Bruner2007-148)[[146]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-149)[[53]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Leonard_2007-55) Fossilized skulls shows the brain size in early humans fell within the range of modern humans 300,000 years ago, but only got its present-day brain shape between 100,000 and 35,000 years ago.[[147]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-150)

[![Image 15: Three students hold three different skulls in front of their faces, to show the difference in size and shape compared to the modern head](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Students_explore_hominid_evolution.jpg/250px-Students_explore_hominid_evolution.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Students_explore_hominid_evolution.jpg)

The size and shape of the skull changed over time. The leftmost, and largest, is a replica of a modern human skull.

The [temporal lobes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Temporal_lobe "Temporal lobe"), which contain centers for language processing, have increased disproportionately, as has the [prefrontal cortex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prefrontal_cortex "Prefrontal cortex"), which has been related to complex decision-making and moderating social behavior.[[141]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Schoeneman-144) Encephalization has been tied to increased starches[[52]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-NYT-20150813-54) and meat[[148]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-151)[[149]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-152) in the diet, however a 2022 meta study called into question the role of meat.[[150]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-153) Other factors are the development of cooking,[[151]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Organ_2011-154) and it has been proposed that intelligence increased as a response to an increased necessity for [solving social problems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dunbar%27s_number "Dunbar's number") as human society became more complex.[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-David-Barrett-155) Changes in skull morphology, such as smaller mandibles and mandible muscle attachments, allowed more room for the brain to grow.[[153]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBownRose1987-156)

The increase in volume of the [neocortex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neocortex "Neocortex") also included a rapid increase in size of the [cerebellum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cerebellum "Cerebellum"). Its function has traditionally been associated with balance and fine motor control, but more recently with [speech](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speech "Speech") and [cognition](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognition "Cognition"). The great apes, including hominids, had a more pronounced cerebellum relative to the neocortex than other primates. It has been suggested that because of its function of sensory-motor control and learning complex muscular actions, the cerebellum may have underpinned human technological adaptations, including the preconditions of speech.[[154]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-157)[[155]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-158)[[156]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-159)[[157]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-160)

The immediate survival advantage of encephalization is difficult to discern, as the major brain changes from _Homo erectus_ to _Homo heidelbergensis_ were not accompanied by major changes in technology. It has been suggested that the changes were mainly social and behavioural, including increased empathic abilities,[[158]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-161)[[159]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-162) increases in size of social groups,[[152]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-David-Barrett-155)[[160]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-163)[[161]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-164) and increased behavioral plasticity.[[162]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-165) Humans are unique in the ability to acquire information through social transmission and adapt that information.[[163]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-166) The emerging field of [cultural evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cultural_evolution "Cultural evolution") studies human sociocultural change from an evolutionary perspective.[[164]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-167)

[![Image 16](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Homo_skull_changes.png/960px-Homo_skull_changes.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Homo_skull_changes.png)
