Evolution of the shape, size, and contours of the human (_Homo_) skull[[165]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-168)[[166]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-169)[[a]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-habilis-12)[[169]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-172)[[d]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-174)[[3]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-:3-3)[[171]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Zeitoun_2003_148%E2%80%93156-175)[[172]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Proceedings_2015-176)[[173]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-:2-177)[[174]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-178)[[108]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-:1-110)[[175]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-179)[[176]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-180)

The reduced degree of [sexual dimorphism in humans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism_in_humans "Sexual dimorphism in humans") is visible primarily in the reduction of the male [canine tooth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canine_tooth "Canine tooth") relative to other ape species (except gibbons) and reduced brow ridges and general robustness of males. Another important physiological change related to [sexuality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexuality "Sexuality") in humans was the evolution of [hidden estrus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Concealed_ovulation "Concealed ovulation"). Humans are the only hominoids in which the female is fertile year round and in which no special signals of fertility are produced by the body (such as genital swelling or overt changes in proceptivity during estrus).[[177]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-181)

Nonetheless, humans retain a degree of sexual dimorphism in the distribution of body hair and subcutaneous fat, and in the overall size, males being around 15% larger than females.[[178]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-182) These changes taken together have been interpreted as a result of an increased emphasis on [pair bonding](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pair_bond "Pair bond") as a possible solution to the requirement for increased parental investment due to the prolonged infancy of offspring.[[179]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-183)

[![Image 17](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Erpurua_eta_hatz_txikia.jpg/120px-Erpurua_eta_hatz_txikia.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Erpurua_eta_hatz_txikia.jpg)

Only the human is able to touch the little finger with the thumb.

The ulnar opposition—the contact between the thumb and the tip of the [little finger](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Little_finger "Little finger") of the same hand—is unique to the [genus _Homo_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homo "Homo"),[[180]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-184) including Neanderthals, the [Sima de los Huesos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atapuerca_Mountains "Atapuerca Mountains")[hominins](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hominini "Hominini") and [anatomically modern humans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homo_sapiens "Homo sapiens").[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Miriam-185)[[182]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-186) In other primates, the thumb is short and unable to touch the little finger.[[181]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Miriam-185) The ulnar opposition facilitates the precision grip and power grip of the human hand, underlying all the skilled manipulations.

A number of other changes have also characterized the evolution of humans, among them an increased reliance on vision rather than smell (highly reduced [olfactory bulb](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olfactory_bulb "Olfactory bulb")); a longer juvenile developmental period and higher infant dependency;[[183]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Kuzawa2014-187) a smaller gut and small, misaligned teeth; faster basal metabolism;[[184]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Pontzer2016-188) loss of body hair;[[185]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-MarkusJRantala2007-189) an increase in [eccrine sweat gland](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eccrine_sweat_gland "Eccrine sweat gland") density that is ten times higher than any other [catarrhinian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Catarrhini "Catarrhini") primates,[[186]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-190) yet humans use 30% to 50% less water per day compared to chimps and gorillas;[[187]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-191) more [REM sleep](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rapid_eye_movement_sleep "Rapid eye movement sleep") but less sleep in total;[[188]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-192) a change in the shape of the dental arcade from u-shaped to parabolic; development of a [chin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chin "Chin") (found in _Homo sapiens_ alone); [styloid processes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Temporal_styloid_process "Temporal styloid process"); and a descended [larynx](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Larynx "Larynx"). As the human hand and arms adapted to the making of tools and were used less for climbing, the shoulder blades changed too. As a side effect, it allowed human ancestors to throw objects with greater force, speed and accuracy.[[189]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-193)

[![Image 18](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Pierre_taill%C3%A9e_Melka_Kunture_%C3%89thiopie_fond.jpg/250px-Pierre_taill%C3%A9e_Melka_Kunture_%C3%89thiopie_fond.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Pierre_taill%C3%A9e_Melka_Kunture_%C3%89thiopie_fond.jpg)

"A sharp rock", an [Oldowan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oldowan "Oldowan") pebble tool, the most basic of human stone tools

[![Image 19](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4e/Small_bonfire.JPG/250px-Small_bonfire.JPG)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Small_bonfire.JPG)

The harnessing of fire was a pivotal milestone in human history.

[![Image 20](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/81/Acheuleanhandaxes.jpg/250px-Acheuleanhandaxes.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Acheuleanhandaxes.jpg)

[Acheulean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acheulean "Acheulean") hand-axes from [Kent](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kent "Kent"). _H.erectus_[flint](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flint "Flint") work. The types shown are (clockwise from top) cordate, ficron and ovate.

[![Image 21](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/19/Venus_of_Willendorf_frontview_retouched_2.jpg/250px-Venus_of_Willendorf_frontview_retouched_2.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Venus_of_Willendorf_frontview_retouched_2.jpg)

[Venus of Willendorf](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Venus_of_Willendorf "Venus of Willendorf"), an example of [Paleolithic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paleolithic "Paleolithic") art, dated circa 30,000 years ago[[190]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-PHYS-194)

The use of tools has been interpreted as a sign of intelligence, and it has been theorized that tool use may have stimulated certain aspects of human evolution, especially the continued expansion of the human brain.[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-auto-195) Paleontology has yet to explain the expansion of this organ over millions of years despite being extremely demanding in terms of energy consumption. The brain of a modern human consumes, on average, about 13 watts (260 kilocalories per day), a fifth of the body's resting power consumption.[[192]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-196) Increased tool use would allow hunting for energy-rich meat products, and would enable processing more energy-rich plant products. Researchers have suggested that early hominins were thus under evolutionary pressure to increase their capacity to create and use tools.[[193]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-197)

Precisely when early humans started to use tools is difficult to determine, because the more primitive these tools are (for example, sharp-edged stones) the more difficult it is to decide whether they are natural objects or human artifacts.[[191]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-auto-195) There is some evidence that the australopithecines (4 Ma) [may have used broken bones as tools](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Osteodontokeratic_culture "Osteodontokeratic culture"), but this is debated.[[194]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTERobinson2008[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid-kOvkb-xbU8CpgPA398_398]-198)

[Many species make and use tools](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tool_use_by_animals "Tool use by animals"), but it is the human genus that dominates the areas of making and using more complex tools. The oldest known tools are flakes from West Turkana, Kenya, which date to 3.3 million years ago.[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Harmand_310%E2%80%93315-199) The next oldest stone tools are from [Gona, Ethiopia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gona,_Ethiopia "Gona, Ethiopia"), and are considered the beginning of the Oldowan technology. These tools date to about 2.6 million years ago.[[196]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-200) A _Homo_ fossil was found near some [Oldowan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oldowan "Oldowan") tools, and its age was noted at 2.3 million years old, suggesting that maybe the _Homo_ species did indeed create and use these tools. It is a possibility but does not yet represent solid evidence.[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreemanHerron2007786%E2%80%93788-201) The [third metacarpal styloid process](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Third_metacarpal_styloid_process "Third metacarpal styloid process") enables the hand bone to lock into the wrist bones, allowing for greater amounts of pressure to be applied to the wrist and hand from a grasping thumb and fingers. It allows humans the dexterity and strength to make and use complex tools. This unique anatomical feature separates humans from other apes and other nonhuman primates, and is not seen in human fossils older than 1.8 million years.[[198]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-202)

Bernard Wood noted that _Paranthropus_ co-existed with the early _Homo_ species in the area of the "Oldowan Industrial Complex" over roughly the same span of time. Although there is no direct evidence which identifies _Paranthropus_ as the tool makers, their anatomy lends to indirect evidence of their capabilities in this area. Most paleoanthropologists agree that the early _Homo_ species were indeed responsible for most of the Oldowan tools found. They argue that when most of the Oldowan tools were found in association with human fossils, _Homo_ was always present, but _Paranthropus_ was not.[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreemanHerron2007786%E2%80%93788-201)

In 1994, Randall Susman used the anatomy of opposable thumbs as the basis for his argument that both the _Homo_ and _Paranthropus_ species were toolmakers. He compared bones and muscles of human and chimpanzee thumbs, finding that humans have 3 muscles which are lacking in chimpanzees. Humans also have thicker metacarpals with broader heads, allowing more precise grasping than the chimpanzee hand can perform. Susman posited that modern anatomy of the human opposable thumb is an evolutionary response to the requirements associated with making and handling tools and that both species were indeed toolmakers.[[197]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEFreemanHerron2007786%E2%80%93788-201)

Transition to behavioral modernity
----------------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&action=edit&section=23 "Edit section: Transition to behavioral modernity")]

Anthropologists describe [modern human behavior](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Modern_human_behavior "Modern human behavior") to include cultural and behavioral traits such as specialization of tools, use of jewellery and images (such as cave drawings), organization of living space, rituals (such as grave gifts), specialized hunting techniques, exploration of less hospitable geographical areas, and [barter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Barter "Barter") trade networks, as well as more general traits such as language and complex symbolic thinking. Debate continues as to whether a "revolution" led to modern humans ("big bang of human consciousness"), or whether the evolution was more gradual.[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Mcbrearty_Brooks-50)

Until about 50,000–40,000 years ago, the use of stone tools seems to have progressed stepwise. Each phase (_H.habilis_, _H.ergaster_, _H.neanderthalensis_) marked a new technology, followed by very slow development until the next phase. Currently paleoanthropologists are debating whether these _Homo_ species possessed some or many modern human behaviors. They seem to have been culturally conservative, maintaining the same technologies and foraging patterns over very long periods.

Around 50,000 [BP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Before_Present "Before Present"), human culture started to evolve more rapidly. The transition to behavioral modernity has been characterized by some as a "**Great Leap Forward**",[[199]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDiamond199939-203) or as the "Upper Palaeolithic Revolution",[[200]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-204) due to the sudden appearance in the archaeological record of distinctive signs of modern behavior and [big game hunting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Big_game_hunting "Big game hunting").[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-oppenheimer-205) Evidence of behavioral modernity significantly earlier also exists from Africa, with older evidence of abstract imagery, widened subsistence strategies, more sophisticated tools and weapons, and other "modern" behaviors, and many scholars have recently argued that the transition to modernity occurred sooner than previously believed.[[48]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Mcbrearty_Brooks-50)[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Henshilwood_etal_2002-206)[[203]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Henshilwood_Marean_2003-207)[[204]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Backwell-208)

Other scholars consider the transition to have been more gradual, noting that some features had already appeared among archaic African _Homo sapiens_ 300,000–200,000 years ago.[[205]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-209)[[206]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-210)[[207]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-211)[[208]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-The_Atlantic-555674-212)[[209]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Brooks-213) Recent evidence suggests that the Australian Aboriginal population separated from the African population 75,000 years ago, and that they made a 160 km (99 mi) sea journey 60,000 years ago, which may diminish the significance of the Upper Paleolithic Revolution.[[210]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-214)

Modern humans started burying their dead, making clothing from animal hides, hunting with more sophisticated techniques (such as using [pit traps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trapping_pit "Trapping pit") or driving animals off cliffs), and [cave painting](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cave_painting "Cave painting").[[211]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-215) As human culture advanced, different populations innovated existing technologies: artifacts such as fish hooks, buttons, and bone needles show signs of cultural variation, which had not been seen prior to 50,000 BP. Typically, the older _H.neanderthalensis_ populations did not vary in their technologies, although the [Chatelperronian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chatelperronian "Chatelperronian") assemblages have been found to be Neanderthal imitations of _H.sapiens_[Aurignacian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aurignacian "Aurignacian") technologies.[[212]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-216)

Recent and ongoing human evolution
----------------------------------

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&action=edit&section=24 "Edit section: Recent and ongoing human evolution")]

[Anatomically modern human](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatomically_modern_human "Anatomically modern human") populations continue to evolve, as they are affected by both natural selection and [genetic drift](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetic_drift "Genetic drift"). Although [selection pressure](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Selection_pressure "Selection pressure") on some traits, such as resistance to smallpox, has decreased in the modern age, humans are still undergoing natural selection for many other traits. Some of these are due to specific environmental pressures, while others are related to lifestyle changes since the development of agriculture (10,000 years ago), urbanization (5,000), and industrialization (250 years ago). It has been argued that human evolution has accelerated since the development of agriculture 10,000 years ago and civilization some 5,000 years ago, resulting, it is claimed, in substantial genetic differences between different current human populations,[[213]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTECochranHarpending2009-217) and more recent research indicates that for some traits, the developments and innovations of human culture have driven a new form of selection that coexists with, and in some cases has largely replaced, natural selection.[[214]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-218)

[![Image 22](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e8/Homo_sapiens_sapiens%2C_Oase%2C_Rum%C3%A4nien_%28Daniela_Hitzemann%29.jpg/250px-Homo_sapiens_sapiens%2C_Oase%2C_Rum%C3%A4nien_%28Daniela_Hitzemann%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Homo_sapiens_sapiens,_Oase,_Rum%C3%A4nien_(Daniela_Hitzemann).jpg)

Reconstruction of the [upper Palaeolithic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Initial_Upper_Paleolithic "Initial Upper Paleolithic") human [Oase 2](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oase_2 "Oase 2")c. 40 000 years BP[[215]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-219)

Particularly conspicuous is variation in superficial characteristics, such as [Afro-textured hair](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afro-textured_hair "Afro-textured hair"), or the recent evolution of [light skin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Light_skin "Light skin") and blond hair in some populations, which are attributed to differences in climate. Particularly strong selective pressures have resulted in [high-altitude adaptation in humans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High-altitude_adaptation_in_humans "High-altitude adaptation in humans"), with different ones in different isolated populations. Studies of the [genetic basis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/High-altitude_adaptation_in_humans#Genetic_basis "High-altitude adaptation in humans") show that some developed very recently, with Tibetans evolving over 3,000 years to have high proportions of an allele of [EPAS1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/EPAS1 "EPAS1") that is adaptive to high altitudes.

Other evolution is related to [endemic diseases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endemic_disease "Endemic disease"): the presence of [malaria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Malaria "Malaria") selects for [sickle cell trait](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sickle_cell_trait "Sickle cell trait") (the [heterozygous](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heterozygous "Heterozygous") form of sickle cell gene), while in the absence of malaria, the health effects of [sickle-cell anemia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sickle-cell_anemia "Sickle-cell anemia") select against this trait. For another example, the population at risk of the severe debilitating disease [kuru](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kuru_(disease) "Kuru (disease)") has significant over-representation of an immune variant of the [prion protein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/PRNP "PRNP") gene G127V versus non-immune alleles. The frequency of this [genetic variant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mutation "Mutation") is due to the survival of immune persons.[[216]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-220)[[217]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-221) Some reported trends remain unexplained and the subject of ongoing research in the novel field of evolutionary medicine: [polycystic ovary syndrome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Polycystic_ovary_syndrome "Polycystic ovary syndrome") (PCOS) reduces fertility and thus is expected to be subject to extremely strong negative selection, but its relative commonality in human populations suggests a counteracting selection pressure. The identity of that pressure remains the subject of some debate.[[218]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-222)

Recent human evolution related to agriculture includes genetic resistance to infectious disease that has appeared in human populations by crossing the species barrier from domesticated animals,[[219]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDiamond1999-223) as well as changes in metabolism due to changes in diet, such as [lactase persistence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lactase_persistence "Lactase persistence").

Culturally-driven evolution can defy the expectations of natural selection: while human populations experience some pressure that drives a selection for producing children at younger ages, the advent of effective contraception, higher education, and changing social norms have driven the observed selection in the opposite direction.[[220]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-224) However, culturally-driven selection need not necessarily work counter or in opposition to natural selection: some proposals to explain the high rate of recent human brain expansion indicate a kind of feedback whereupon the brain's increased social learning efficiency encourages cultural developments that in turn encourage more efficiency, which drive more complex cultural developments that demand still-greater efficiency, and so forth.[[221]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-225) Culturally-driven evolution has an advantage in that in addition to the genetic effects, it can be observed also in the archaeological record: the development of stone tools across the Palaeolithic period connects to culturally-driven cognitive development in the form of skill acquisition supported by the culture and the development of increasingly complex technologies and the cognitive ability to elaborate them.[[222]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-226)

In contemporary times, since industrialization, some trends have been observed: for instance, menopause is evolving to occur later.[[223]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-doi10.1073/pnas.0906199106-227) Other reported trends appear to include lengthening of the human reproductive period and reduction in cholesterol levels, blood glucose and blood pressure in some populations.[[223]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-doi10.1073/pnas.0906199106-227)

The name _Homo_ of the biological genus to which humans belong is [Latin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Latin "Latin") for 'human'.[[e]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-228) It was chosen originally by [Carl Linnaeus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus "Carl Linnaeus") in his classification system.[[f]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-229) The English word _human_ is from the Latin _humanus_, the adjectival form of _homo_. The Latin _homo_ derives from the [Indo-European](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language "Proto-Indo-European language") root *_dhghem_, or 'earth'.[[224]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-230) Linnaeus and other scientists of his time also considered the great apes to be the closest relatives of humans based on [morphological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Morphology_(biology) "Morphology (biology)") and [anatomical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatomy "Anatomy") similarities.[[225]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-231)

The possibility of linking humans with earlier apes by descent became clear only after 1859 with the publication of [Charles Darwin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Darwin "Charles Darwin")'s _[On the Origin of Species](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/On\_the\_Origin\_of\_Species "On the Origin of Species")_, in which he argued for the idea of the evolution of new species from earlier ones. Darwin's book did not address the question of human evolution, saying only that "Light will be thrown on the origin of man and his history."[[226]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDarwin1859[httpdarwin-onlineorgukcontentframesetitemIDF373viewtypetextpageseq506_367%E2%80%93404]-232)

The first debates about the nature of human evolution arose between [Thomas Henry Huxley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Henry_Huxley "Thomas Henry Huxley") and [Richard Owen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Owen "Richard Owen"). Huxley argued for human evolution from apes by illustrating many of the similarities and differences between humans and other apes, and did so particularly in his 1863 book _[Evidence as to Man's Place in Nature](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evidence\_as\_to\_Man%27s\_Place\_in\_Nature "Evidence as to Man's Place in Nature")_. Many of Darwin's early supporters (such as [Alfred Russel Wallace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfred_Russel_Wallace "Alfred Russel Wallace") and [Charles Lyell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Lyell "Charles Lyell")) did not initially agree that the origin of the mental capacities and the moral sensibilities of humans could be explained by [natural selection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_selection "Natural selection"), though this later changed. Darwin applied the theory of evolution and [sexual selection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexual_selection "Sexual selection") to humans in his 1871 book _[The Descent of Man, and Selection in Relation to Sex](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The\_Descent\_of\_Man,\_and\_Selection\_in\_Relation\_to\_Sex "The Descent of Man, and Selection in Relation to Sex")_.[[227]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDarwin1981-233)

A major problem in the 19th century was the lack of [fossil intermediaries](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transitional_fossil "Transitional fossil"). [Neanderthal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neanderthal "Neanderthal") remains were discovered in a limestone quarry in 1856, three years before the publication of _On the Origin of Species_, and Neanderthal fossils had been discovered in Gibraltar even earlier, but it was originally claimed that these were the remains of a modern human who had suffered some kind of illness.[[228]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMontgomery198895%E2%80%9396-234) Despite the 1891 discovery by [Eugène Dubois](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eug%C3%A8ne_Dubois "Eugène Dubois") of what is now called _Homo erectus_ at [Trinil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trinil "Trinil"), [Java](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Java "Java"), it was only in the 1920s when such fossils were discovered in Africa, that intermediate species began to accumulate.[[229]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-235) In 1925, [Raymond Dart](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Raymond_Dart "Raymond Dart") described _[Australopithecus africanus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_africanus "Australopithecus africanus")_.[[230]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-236) The [type specimen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Type_(biology) "Type (biology)") was the [Taung Child](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Taung_Child "Taung Child"), an australopithecine infant which was discovered in a cave. The child's remains were a remarkably well-preserved tiny skull and an [endocast](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endocast "Endocast") of the brain.

Although the brain was small (410 cm 3), its shape was rounded, unlike that of chimpanzees and gorillas, and more like a modern human brain. Also, the specimen showed short [canine teeth](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canine_tooth "Canine tooth"), and the position of the [foramen magnum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Foramen_magnum "Foramen magnum") (the hole in the skull where the spine enters) was evidence of [bipedal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bipedal "Bipedal") locomotion. All of these traits convinced Dart that the Taung Child was a bipedal human ancestor, a transitional form between apes and humans.

### The East African fossils

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&action=edit&section=29 "Edit section: The East African fossils")]

[![Image 23](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ab/Fossil_hominids.jpg/250px-Fossil_hominids.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Fossil_hominids.jpg)

[Fossil](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fossil "Fossil")[hominid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hominid "Hominid") evolution display at [The Museum of Osteology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Museum_of_Osteology "The Museum of Osteology"), [Oklahoma City](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oklahoma_City "Oklahoma City"), Oklahoma, US

During the 1960s and 1970s, hundreds of fossils were found in East Africa in the regions of the [Olduvai Gorge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Olduvai_Gorge "Olduvai Gorge") and [Lake Turkana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lake_Turkana "Lake Turkana"). These searches were carried out by the Leakey family, with [Louis Leakey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Louis_Leakey "Louis Leakey") and his wife [Mary Leakey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mary_Leakey "Mary Leakey"), and later their son [Richard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Leakey "Richard Leakey") and daughter-in-law [Meave](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Meave_Leakey "Meave Leakey"), fossil hunters and paleoanthropologists. From the fossil beds of Olduvai and Lake Turkana they amassed specimens of the early hominins: the australopithecines and _Homo_ species, and even _H.erectus_.

These finds cemented Africa as the cradle of humankind. In the late 1970s and the 1980s, [Ethiopia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethiopia "Ethiopia") emerged as the new hot spot of paleoanthropology after ["Lucy"](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lucy_(Australopithecus) "Lucy (Australopithecus)"), the most complete fossil member of the species _[Australopithecus afarensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_afarensis "Australopithecus afarensis")_, was found in 1974 by [Donald Johanson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Johanson "Donald Johanson") near [Hadar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hadar,_Ethiopia "Hadar, Ethiopia") in the desertic [Afar Triangle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afar_Triangle "Afar Triangle") region of northern Ethiopia. Although the specimen had a small brain, the pelvis and leg bones were almost identical in function to those of modern humans, showing with certainty that these hominins had walked erect.[[231]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohansonEdey198120%E2%80%9322,_184%E2%80%93185-237) Lucy was classified as a new species, _Australopithecus afarensis_, which is thought to be more closely related to the genus _Homo_ as a direct ancestor, or as a close relative of an unknown ancestor, than any other known hominid or hominin from this early time range.[[232]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-238) (The specimen was nicknamed "Lucy" after [the Beatles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Beatles "The Beatles")' song "[Lucy in the Sky with Diamonds](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lucy_in_the_Sky_with_Diamonds "Lucy in the Sky with Diamonds")", which was played loudly and repeatedly in the camp during the excavations.)[[233]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohansonEdey198122-239) The [Afar Triangle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Afar_Triangle "Afar Triangle") area would later yield discovery of many more hominin fossils, particularly those uncovered or described by teams headed by [Tim D. White](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tim_D._White "Tim D. White") in the 1990s, including _[Ardipithecus ramidus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ardipithecus\_ramidus "Ardipithecus ramidus")_ and _[A.kadabba](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ardipithecus\_kadabba "Ardipithecus kadabba")_.[[234]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-240)

In 2013, fossil skeletons of _[Homo naledi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homo\_naledi "Homo naledi")_, an [extinct species](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extinct_species "Extinct species") of [hominin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hominin "Hominin") assigned (provisionally) to the genus _Homo_, were found in the [Rising Star Cave](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Rising_Star_Cave "Rising Star Cave") system, a site in South Africa's [Cradle of Humankind](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cradle_of_Humankind "Cradle of Humankind") region in [Gauteng](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gauteng "Gauteng") province near [Johannesburg](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johannesburg "Johannesburg").[[235]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-241)[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-NGS-20150910-242) As of September 2015, fossils of at least fifteen individuals, amounting to 1,550 specimens, have been excavated from the cave.[[236]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-NGS-20150910-242) The species is characterized by a body mass and stature similar to small-bodied human populations, a smaller [endocranial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Endocranium "Endocranium") volume similar to _[Australopithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus "Australopithecus")_, and a [cranial](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skull "Skull")[morphology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Morphology_(biology) "Morphology (biology)") (skull shape) similar to early _Homo_ species. The skeletal anatomy combines primitive features known from [australopithecines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecine "Australopithecine") with features known from early hominins. The individuals show signs of having been deliberately disposed of within the cave near the time of death. The fossils were dated close to 250,000 years ago,[[237]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-243) and thus are not ancestral but contemporary with the first appearance of larger-brained [anatomically modern humans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anatomically_modern_humans "Anatomically modern humans").[[238]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-eLIFE-2017a-244)

### The genetic revolution

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&action=edit&section=30 "Edit section: The genetic revolution")]

The genetic revolution in studies of human evolution started when [Vincent Sarich](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vincent_Sarich "Vincent Sarich") and [Allan Wilson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allan_Wilson_(biologist) "Allan Wilson (biologist)") measured the strength of immunological cross-reactions of [blood serum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Serum_(blood) "Serum (blood)")[albumin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Albumin "Albumin") between pairs of creatures, including humans and African apes (chimpanzees and gorillas).[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-MolClock-245) The strength of the reaction could be expressed numerically as an immunological distance, which was in turn proportional to the number of [amino acid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Amino_acid "Amino acid") differences between homologous proteins in different species. By constructing a calibration curve of the ID of species' pairs with known divergence times in the fossil record, the data could be used as a [molecular clock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_clock "Molecular clock") to estimate the times of divergence of pairs with poorer or unknown fossil records.

In their seminal 1967 paper in _[Science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Science\_(journal) "Science (journal)")_, Sarich and Wilson estimated the divergence time of humans and apes as four to five million years ago,[[239]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-MolClock-245) at a time when standard interpretations of the fossil record gave this divergence as at least 10 to as much as 30 million years. Subsequent fossil discoveries, notably "Lucy", and reinterpretation of older fossil materials, notably _[Ramapithecus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sivapithecus "Sivapithecus")_, showed the younger estimates to be correct and validated the albumin method.

Progress in [DNA sequencing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA_sequencing "DNA sequencing"), specifically [mitochondrial DNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA "Mitochondrial DNA") (mtDNA) and then [Y-chromosome DNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_Y-chromosome_DNA_haplogroup "Human Y-chromosome DNA haplogroup") (Y-DNA) advanced the understanding of human origins.[[126]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDeSalleTattersall2008[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidBf4Sitw7YaICpgPA146_146]-128)[[240]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEM'charek2005[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidn-K436zeUY8CpgPA96_96]-246)[[241]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTETrent2005[httpsbooksgooglecombooksid772BEp8ZyMYCpgPA6_6]-247) Application of the [molecular clock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_clock "Molecular clock") principle revolutionized the study of [molecular evolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_evolution "Molecular evolution").

On the basis of a separation from the [orangutan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orangutan "Orangutan") between 10 and 20 million years ago, earlier studies of the molecular clock suggested that there were about 76 mutations per generation that were not inherited by human children from their parents; this evidence supported the divergence time between hominins and chimpanzees noted above. However, a 2012 study in Iceland of 78 children and their parents suggests a mutation rate of only 36 mutations per generation; this datum extends the separation between humans and chimpanzees to an earlier period greater than 7 million years ago ([Ma](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Year#SI_prefix_multipliers "Year")). Additional research with 226 offspring of wild chimpanzee populations in eight locations suggests that chimpanzees reproduce at age 26.5 years on average; which suggests the human divergence from chimpanzees occurred between 7 and 13 mya. And these data suggest that _Ardipithecus_ (4.5 Ma), _Orrorin_ (6 Ma) and _Sahelanthropus_ (7 Ma) all may be on the hominid [lineage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lineage_(evolution) "Lineage (evolution)"), and even that the separation may have occurred outside the [East African Rift](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/East_African_Rift "East African Rift") region.

Furthermore, analysis of the two species' genes in 2006 provides evidence that after human ancestors had started to diverge from chimpanzees, interspecies mating between "proto-human" and "proto-chimpanzees" nonetheless occurred regularly enough to change certain genes in the new [gene pool](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gene_pool "Gene pool"):

A new comparison of the human and chimpanzee genomes suggests that after the two lineages separated, they may have begun interbreeding... A principal finding is that the [X chromosomes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/X_chromosome "X chromosome") of humans and chimpanzees appear to have [diverged](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetic_divergence "Genetic divergence") about 1.2 million years more recently than the other chromosomes.
The research suggests:

There were in fact two splits between the human and chimpanzee lineages, with the first being followed by interbreeding between the two populations and then a second split. The suggestion of a hybridization has startled paleoanthropologists, who nonetheless are treating the new genetic data seriously.[[242]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-248)

### The quest for the earliest hominin

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&action=edit&section=31 "Edit section: The quest for the earliest hominin")]

In the 1990s, several teams of paleoanthropologists were working throughout Africa looking for evidence of the earliest divergence of the hominin lineage from the great apes. In 1994, Meave Leakey discovered _[Australopithecus anamensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_anamensis "Australopithecus anamensis")_. The find was overshadowed by Tim D. White's 1995 discovery of _Ardipithecus ramidus_, which pushed back the fossil record to [4.2](https://geoltime.github.io/?Ma=4.2)million years ago.

In 2000, [Martin Pickford](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Martin_Pickford "Martin Pickford") and [Brigitte Senut](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brigitte_Senut "Brigitte Senut") discovered, in the [Tugen Hills](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tugen_Hills "Tugen Hills") of [Kenya](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kenya "Kenya"), a 6-million-year-old bipedal hominin which they named _[Orrorin tugenensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orrorin "Orrorin")_. And in 2001, a team led by [Michel Brunet](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Michel_Brunet_(paleontologist) "Michel Brunet (paleontologist)") discovered the skull of _[Sahelanthropus tchadensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sahelanthropus "Sahelanthropus")_ which was dated as [7.2](https://geoltime.github.io/?Ma=7.2) million years ago, and which Brunet argued was a bipedal, and therefore a hominid—that is, a hominin (_cf_[Hominidae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hominidae "Hominidae"); terms "hominids" and hominins).

[![Image 24: Trellis of intermingling populations for the last two million years.](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/6/66/Homo_trellis.jpg/250px-Homo_trellis.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Homo_trellis.jpg)

A "trellis" (as [Milford H. Wolpoff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milford_H._Wolpoff "Milford H. Wolpoff") called it) that emphasizes back-and-forth gene flow among geographic regions[[246]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-252)

Different models for the beginning of the present human species

Anthropologists in the 1980s were divided regarding some details of reproductive barriers and migratory dispersals of the genus _Homo_. Subsequently, genetics has been used to investigate and resolve these issues. According to the [Sahara pump theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sahara_pump_theory "Sahara pump theory") evidence suggests that the genus _Homo_ have migrated out of Africa at least three and possibly four times (e.g. _Homo erectus_, _Homo heidelbergensis_ and two or three times for _Homo sapiens_). Recent evidence suggests these dispersals are closely related to fluctuating periods of climate change.[[247]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-253)

Recent evidence suggests that humans may have left Africa half a million years earlier than previously thought. A joint Franco-Indian team has found human artifacts in the Siwalk Hills north of New Delhi dating back at least 2.6 million years. This is earlier than the previous earliest finding of genus _Homo_ at [Dmanisi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dmanisi "Dmanisi"), in [Georgia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Georgia_(country) "Georgia (country)"), dating to 1.85 million years. Although controversial, tools found at a Chinese cave strengthen the case that humans used tools as far back as 2.48 million years ago.[[248]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-254) This suggests that the Asian "Chopper" tool tradition, found in Java and northern China may have left Africa before the appearance of the [Acheulian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acheulian "Acheulian") hand axe.

#### Dispersal of modern _Homo sapiens_

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&action=edit&section=33 "Edit section: Dispersal of modern Homo sapiens")]

Up until the genetic evidence became available, there were two dominant models for the dispersal of modern humans. The [multiregional hypothesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Multiregional_evolution "Multiregional evolution") proposed that the genus _Homo_ contained only a single interconnected population as it does today (not separate species), and that its evolution took place worldwide continuously over the last couple of million years. This model was proposed in 1988 by [Milford H. Wolpoff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Milford_H._Wolpoff "Milford H. Wolpoff").[[249]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-255)[[250]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-256) In contrast, the "out of Africa" model proposed that modern _H.sapiens_ speciated in Africa recently (that is, approximately 200,000 years ago) and the subsequent migration through [Eurasia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Eurasia "Eurasia") resulted in the nearly complete replacement of other _Homo_ species. This model has been developed by [Chris Stringer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chris_Stringer "Chris Stringer") and Peter Andrews.[[251]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-257)[[252]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-258)

Sequencing mtDNA and Y-DNA sampled from a wide range of indigenous populations revealed ancestral information relating to both male and female genetic heritage, and strengthened the "out of Africa" theory and weakened the views of multiregional evolutionism.[[253]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWebster2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidcdiy1EhJVW0CpgPA53_53]-259) Aligned in genetic tree differences were interpreted as supportive of a recent single origin.[[254]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTERamachandranTangGutenkunstBustamante2010[httpsbooksgooglecombooksidFlfPSpBvKLgCpgPA606_606]-260)

"Out of Africa" has thus gained much support from research using female mitochondrial DNA and the male [Y chromosome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Y_chromosome "Y chromosome"). After analysing genealogy trees constructed using 133 types of mtDNA, researchers concluded that all were descended from a female African progenitor, dubbed [Mitochondrial Eve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mitochondrial_Eve "Mitochondrial Eve"). "Out of Africa" is also supported by the fact that mitochondrial genetic diversity is highest among African populations.[[255]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Cann-261)

A broad study of African genetic diversity, headed by [Sarah Tishkoff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sarah_Tishkoff "Sarah Tishkoff"), found the [San people](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/San_people "San people") had the greatest genetic diversity among the 113 distinct populations sampled, making them one of 14 "ancestral population clusters". The research also located a possible origin of modern human migration in southwestern Africa, near the coastal border of [Namibia](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Namibia "Namibia") and [Angola](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Angola "Angola").[[256]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-262) The fossil evidence was insufficient for archaeologist [Richard Leakey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Leakey "Richard Leakey") to resolve the debate about exactly where in Africa modern humans first appeared.[[257]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTELeakey199487%E2%80%9389-263) Studies of [haplogroups](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haplogroup "Haplogroup") in [Y-chromosomal DNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_Y-chromosome_DNA_haplogroup "Human Y-chromosome DNA haplogroup") and [mitochondrial DNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_mitochondrial_DNA_haplogroup "Human mitochondrial DNA haplogroup") have largely supported a recent African origin.[[258]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-264) All the evidence from autosomal DNA also predominantly supports a Recent African origin. However, evidence for [archaic admixture in modern humans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans "Archaic human admixture with modern humans"), both in Africa and later, throughout Eurasia has recently been suggested by a number of studies.[[259]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-265)

Recent sequencing of Neanderthal[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-greenetal-94) and Denisovan[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-pmid21179161-46) genomes shows that some admixture with these populations has occurred. All modern human groups outside Africa have 1–4% or (according to more recent research) about 1.5–2.6% Neanderthal [alleles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allele "Allele") in their genome,[[93]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-A_high-coverage_Neandertal_genome_f-95) and some [Melanesians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Melanesians "Melanesians") have an additional 4–6% of Denisovan alleles. These new results do not contradict the "out of Africa" model, except in its strictest interpretation, although they make the situation more complex. After recovery from a [genetic bottleneck](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetic_bottleneck "Genetic bottleneck") that some researchers speculate might be linked to the [Toba supervolcano catastrophe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Toba_catastrophe_theory "Toba catastrophe theory"), a fairly small group left Africa and interbred with Neanderthals, probably in the Middle East, on the Eurasian steppe or even in North Africa before their departure. Their still predominantly African descendants spread to populate the world. A fraction in turn interbred with Denisovans, probably in southeastern Asia, before populating Melanesia.[[102]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Reich_2011-104)[HLA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_leukocyte_antigen "Human leukocyte antigen") haplotypes of Neanderthal and Denisova origin have been identified in modern Eurasian and Oceanian populations.[[46]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-10.1126/science.1209202-48) The Denisovan [EPAS1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/EPAS1 "EPAS1") gene has also been found in Tibetan populations.[[260]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-266) Studies of the human genome using machine learning have identified additional genetic contributions in Eurasians from an "unknown" ancestral population potentially related to the Neanderthal-Denisovan lineage.[[261]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-267)

[![Image 25](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/27/Spreading_homo_sapiens_la.svg/500px-Spreading_homo_sapiens_la.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Spreading_homo_sapiens_la.svg)

 A map of [early human migrations](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Early_human_migrations "Early human migrations")

There are still differing theories on whether there was a single exodus from Africa or several. A multiple dispersal model involves the Southern Dispersal theory,[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-lahr-268)[[263]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-269)[[264]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-270) which has gained support in recent years from genetic, linguistic and archaeological evidence. In this theory, there was a coastal dispersal of modern humans from the [Horn of Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horn_of_Africa "Horn of Africa") crossing the Bab el Mandib to Yemen at a lower sea level around 70,000 years ago. This group helped to populate Southeast Asia and Oceania, explaining the discovery of early human sites in these areas much earlier than those in the [Levant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Levant "Levant").[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-lahr-268) This group seems to have been dependent upon marine resources for their survival.

[Stephen Oppenheimer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stephen_Oppenheimer "Stephen Oppenheimer") has proposed a second wave of humans may have later dispersed through the Persian Gulf oases, and the Zagros mountains into the Middle East. Alternatively it may have come across the [Sinai Peninsula](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sinai_Peninsula "Sinai Peninsula") into Asia, from shortly after 50,000 yrs BP, resulting in the bulk of the human populations of Eurasia. It has been suggested that this second group possibly possessed a more sophisticated "big game hunting" tool technology and was less dependent on coastal food sources than the original group. Much of the evidence for the first group's expansion would have been destroyed by the rising sea levels at the end of each [glacial maximum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Glacial_maximum "Glacial maximum").[[262]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-lahr-268) The multiple dispersal model is contradicted by studies indicating that the populations of Eurasia and the populations of Southeast Asia and Oceania are all descended from the same mitochondrial DNA L3 [lineages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lineage_(genetic) "Lineage (genetic)"), which support a single migration out of Africa that gave rise to all non-African populations.[[265]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-271)

On the basis of the early date of Badoshan Iranian Aurignacian, Oppenheimer suggests that this second dispersal may have occurred with a pluvial period about 50,000 years before the present, with modern human big-game hunting cultures spreading up the Zagros Mountains, carrying modern human genomes from Oman, throughout the Persian Gulf, northward into Armenia and Anatolia, with a variant travelling south into Israel and to Cyrenicia.[[201]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-oppenheimer-205)

Recent genetic evidence suggests that all modern non-African populations, including those of Eurasia and Oceania, are descended from a single wave that left Africa between 65,000 and 50,000 years ago.[[266]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Posth-272)[[267]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-273)[[268]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-ReferenceC-274)

The evidence on which scientific accounts of human evolution are based comes from many fields of [natural science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_science "Natural science"). The main source of knowledge about the evolutionary process has traditionally been the fossil record, but since the development of genetics beginning in the 1970s, DNA analysis has come to occupy a place of comparable importance. The studies of ontogeny, [phylogeny](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phylogenetics "Phylogenetics") and especially [evolutionary developmental biology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Evolutionary_developmental_biology "Evolutionary developmental biology") of both vertebrates and invertebrates offer considerable insight into the evolution of all life, including how humans evolved. The specific study of the origin and life of humans is [anthropology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anthropology "Anthropology"), particularly paleoanthropology which focuses on the study of human [prehistory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Prehistory "Prehistory").[[269]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStanfordAllenAnt%C3%B3n2009-275)

### Evidence from genetics

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&action=edit&section=35 "Edit section: Evidence from genetics")]

[![Image 26](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7f/Hominidae_chart.svg/380px-Hominidae_chart.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Hominidae_chart.svg)

Family tree showing the [extant](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Extant_taxon "Extant taxon") hominoids: humans (genus _[Homo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homo "Homo")_), [chimpanzees](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chimpanzee "Chimpanzee") and bonobos (genus _[Pan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pan\_(genus) "Pan (genus)")_), [gorillas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gorilla "Gorilla") (genus _Gorilla_), [orangutans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Orangutan "Orangutan") (genus _[Pongo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pongo\_(genus) "Pongo (genus)")_), and gibbons (four genera of the family [Hylobatidae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hylobatidae "Hylobatidae"): _[Hylobates](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hylobates "Hylobates")_, _[Hoolock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hoolock "Hoolock")_, _[Nomascus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nomascus "Nomascus")_, and _[Symphalangus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symphalangus "Symphalangus")_). All except gibbons are hominids.

The closest living relatives of humans are bonobos and chimpanzees (both genus _Pan_) and gorillas (genus _Gorilla_).[[270]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Wood-276) With the sequencing of both the human and chimpanzee genome, as of 2012 estimates of the similarity between their DNA sequences range between 95% and 99%.[[270]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Wood-276)[[271]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-277)[[31]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-sayers-33) It is also noteworthy that mice share around 97.5% of their working DNA with humans.[[272]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-278) By using the technique called the [molecular clock](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Molecular_clock "Molecular clock") which estimates the time required for the number of divergent mutations to accumulate between two lineages, the approximate date for the split between lineages can be calculated.

The gibbons (family Hylobatidae) and then the orangutans (genus _Pongo_) were the first groups to split from the line leading to the hominins, including humans—followed by gorillas (genus _Gorilla_), and, ultimately, by the chimpanzees (genus _Pan_). The splitting date between hominin and chimpanzee lineages is placed by some between [4 to 8](https://geoltime.github.io/?Ma=4%E2%80%938) million years ago, that is, during the [Late Miocene](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Late_Miocene "Late Miocene").[[273]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDawkins2004-279)[[274]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-280)[[275]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-281)[[276]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Ruvolo1997-282) Speciation, however, appears to have been unusually drawn out. Initial divergence occurred sometime between [7 to 13](https://geoltime.github.io/?Ma=7%E2%80%9313) million years ago, but ongoing hybridization blurred the separation and delayed complete separation during several millions of years. Patterson (2006) dated the final divergence at [5 to 6](https://geoltime.github.io/?Ma=5%E2%80%936) million years ago.[[277]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-283)

Genetic evidence has also been employed to compare species within the genus _Homo_, investigating [gene flow between early modern humans and Neanderthals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Archaic_human_admixture_with_modern_humans "Archaic human admixture with modern humans"), and to enhance the understanding of the early human migration patterns and splitting dates. By comparing the parts of the genome that are [not under natural selection](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neutral_theory_of_molecular_evolution "Neutral theory of molecular evolution") and which therefore accumulate mutations at a fairly steady rate, it is possible to reconstruct a genetic tree incorporating the entire human species since the last shared ancestor.

Each time a certain mutation ([single-nucleotide polymorphism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Single-nucleotide_polymorphism "Single-nucleotide polymorphism")) appears in an individual and is passed on to his or her descendants, a haplogroup is formed including all of the descendants of the individual who will also carry that mutation. By comparing mitochondrial [DNA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DNA "DNA") which is inherited only from the mother, geneticists have concluded that the last female common ancestor whose [genetic marker](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genetic_marker "Genetic marker") is found in all modern humans, the so-called [mitochondrial Eve](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mitochondrial_Eve "Mitochondrial Eve"), must have lived around 200,000 years ago.

Human evolutionary genetics studies how [human genomes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_genome "Human genome") differ among individuals, the evolutionary past that gave rise to them, and their current effects. Differences between genomes have [anthropological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Anthropology "Anthropology"), medical and [forensic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Forensic_science "Forensic science") implications and applications. Genetic data can provide important insight into human evolution.

In May 2023, scientists reported a more complicated pathway of human evolution than previously understood. According to the studies, humans evolved from different places and times in Africa, instead of from a single location and period of time.[[278]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-NYT-20230517-284)[[279]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-NAT-20230517-285)

### Evidence from the fossil record

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&action=edit&section=36 "Edit section: Evidence from the fossil record")]

[![Image 27](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/45/Homo_habilis-KNM_ER_1813.jpg/250px-Homo_habilis-KNM_ER_1813.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Homo_habilis-KNM_ER_1813.jpg)

Replica of fossil skull of _[H.habilis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H.\_habilis "H. habilis")_. Fossil number KNM ER 1813, found at [Koobi Fora](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Koobi_Fora "Koobi Fora"), Kenya.

[![Image 28](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/70/Homo_ergaster.jpg/250px-Homo_ergaster.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Homo_ergaster.jpg)

Replica of fossil skull of _[H.ergaster](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H.\_ergaster "H. ergaster")_ (African _H.erectus_). Fossil number Khm-Heu 3733 discovered in 1975 in Kenya.

There is little fossil evidence for the divergence of the gorilla, chimpanzee and hominin lineages.[[280]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-286) The earliest fossils that have been proposed as members of the hominin lineage are _Sahelanthropus tchadensis_ dating from [7](https://geoltime.github.io/?Ma=7)million years ago, _Orrorin tugenensis_ dating from [5.7](https://geoltime.github.io/?Ma=5.7)million years ago, and _Ardipithecus kadabba_ dating to [5.6](https://geoltime.github.io/?Ma=5.6)million years ago. Each of these have been argued to be a [bipedal](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bipedal "Bipedal") ancestor of later hominins but, in each case, the claims have been contested. It is also possible that one or more of these species are ancestors of another branch of African apes, or that they represent a shared ancestor between hominins and other apes.

The question then of the relationship between these early fossil species and the hominin lineage is still to be resolved. From these early species, the australopithecines arose around [4](https://geoltime.github.io/?Ma=4)million years ago and diverged into [robust](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paranthropus "Paranthropus") (also called _[Paranthropus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paranthropus "Paranthropus")_) and [gracile](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus "Australopithecus") branches, one of which (possibly _[A. garhi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Australopithecus\_garhi "Australopithecus garhi")_) probably went on to become ancestors of the genus _Homo_. The australopithecine species that is best represented in the fossil record is _Australopithecus afarensis_ with more than 100 fossil individuals represented, found from Northern Ethiopia (such as the famous "Lucy"), to Kenya, and South Africa. Fossils of robust australopithecines such as _A. robustus_ (or alternatively _[Paranthropus robustus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paranthropus\_robustus "Paranthropus robustus")_) and _A./P. boisei_ are particularly abundant in South Africa at sites such as [Kromdraai](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kromdraai "Kromdraai") and [Swartkrans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Swartkrans "Swartkrans"), and around [Lake Turkana](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lake_Turkana "Lake Turkana") in Kenya.

The earliest member of the genus _Homo_ is _Homo habilis_ which evolved around [2.8](https://geoltime.github.io/?Ma=2.8)million years ago.[[34]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-autogenerated1-36)_H.habilis_ is the first species for which we have positive evidence of the use of stone tools. They developed the [Oldowan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oldowan "Oldowan") lithic technology, named after the Olduvai Gorge in which the first specimens were found. Some scientists consider _[Homo rudolfensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homo\_rudolfensis "Homo rudolfensis")_, a larger bodied group of fossils with similar morphology to the original _H.habilis_ fossils, to be a separate species, while others consider them to be part of _H.habilis_—simply representing intraspecies variation, or perhaps even [sexual dimorphism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Sexual_dimorphism "Sexual dimorphism"). The brains of these early hominins were about the same size as that of a chimpanzee, and their main adaptation was bipedalism as an adaptation to terrestrial living.

During the next million years, a process of encephalization began and, by the arrival (about [1.9](https://geoltime.github.io/?Ma=1.9)million years ago) of _H.erectus_ in the fossil record, cranial capacity had doubled. _H.erectus_ were the first of the hominins to emigrate from Africa, and, from [1.8 to 1.3](https://geoltime.github.io/?Ma=1.8%E2%80%931.3) million years ago, this species spread through Africa, Asia, and Europe. One population of _H.erectus_, also sometimes classified as separate species _H.ergaster_, remained in Africa and evolved into _H.sapiens_. It is believed that _H.erectus_ and _H.ergaster_ were the first to use fire and complex tools. In Eurasia, _H.erectus_ evolved into species such as _[H.antecessor](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H.\_antecessor "H. antecessor")_, _[H.heidelbergensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H.\_heidelbergensis "H. heidelbergensis")_ and _[H.neanderthalensis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H.\_neanderthalensis "H. neanderthalensis")_. The earliest fossils of anatomically modern humans are from the Middle Paleolithic, about 300–200,000 years ago such as the Herto and [Omo remains](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Omo_remains "Omo remains") of Ethiopia, [Jebel Irhoud](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jebel_Irhoud "Jebel Irhoud") remains of Morocco, and Florisbad remains of South Africa; [later fossils](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skhul_and_Qafzeh_hominins "Skhul and Qafzeh hominins") from the [Skhul Cave](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Skhul_Cave "Skhul Cave") in Israel and Southern Europe begin around 90,000 years ago ([0.09](https://geoltime.github.io/?Ma=0.09)million years ago).

As modern humans spread out from Africa, they encountered other hominins such as _H.neanderthalensis_ and the Denisovans, who may have evolved from populations of _H.erectus_ that had left Africa around [2](https://geoltime.github.io/?Ma=2)million years ago. The nature of interaction between early humans and these sister species has been a long-standing source of controversy, the question being whether humans replaced these earlier species or whether they were in fact similar enough to interbreed, in which case these earlier populations may have contributed genetic material to modern humans.[[281]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWood2009[httpslinkspringercomchapter1010072F978-1-4020-9980-9_3page-1_17%E2%80%9327]-287)[[282]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-NYT-01302012-288)

This migration out of Africa is estimated to have begun about 70–50,000 years [BP](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Before_Present "Before Present") and modern humans subsequently spread globally, replacing earlier hominins either through competition or hybridization. They inhabited Eurasia and Oceania by 40,000 years BP, and the Americas by at least 14,500 years BP.[[283]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-289)

### Inter-species breeding

[[edit](https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Human_evolution&action=edit&section=37 "Edit section: Inter-species breeding")]

[![Image 29](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/42/Homo_lineage_2017update.svg/500px-Homo_lineage_2017update.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Homo_lineage_2017update.svg)

A model of the evolution of the genus _Homo_ over the last 2 million years (vertical axis). The rapid "[Out of Africa](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Recent_African_origin_of_modern_humans "Recent African origin of modern humans")" expansion of _H. sapiens_ is indicated at the top of the diagram, with admixture indicated with [Neanderthals](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neanderthals "Neanderthals"), [Denisovans](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Denisovans "Denisovans"), and unspecified archaic African hominins.

The hypothesis of interbreeding, also known as hybridization, admixture or hybrid-origin theory, has been discussed ever since the discovery of Neanderthal remains in the 19th century.[[284]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-290) The linear view of human evolution began to be abandoned in the 1970s as different species of humans were discovered that made the linear concept increasingly unlikely. In the 21st century with the advent of molecular biology techniques and computerization, [whole-genome sequencing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Whole-genome_sequencing "Whole-genome sequencing") of Neanderthal and human [genome](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genome "Genome") were performed, confirming recent admixture between different human species.[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-greenetal-94) In 2010, evidence based on molecular biology was published, revealing unambiguous examples of interbreeding between archaic and modern humans during the [Middle Paleolithic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic "Middle Paleolithic") and early [Upper Paleolithic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic "Upper Paleolithic"). It has been demonstrated that interbreeding happened in several independent events that included Neanderthals and Denisovans, as well as several unidentified hominins.[[285]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-291) Today, approximately 2% of DNA from all non-African populations (including Europeans, Asians, and [Oceanians](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oceanians "Oceanians")) is Neanderthal,[[92]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-greenetal-94) with traces of Denisovan heritage.[[286]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-NG-20180822-292) Also, 4–6% of modern [Melanesian](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Melanesians "Melanesians") genetics are Denisovan.[[286]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-NG-20180822-292) Comparisons of the human genome to the genomes of Neandertals, Denisovans and apes can help identify features that set modern humans apart from other hominin species. In a 2016 [comparative genomics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Comparative_genomics "Comparative genomics") study, a Harvard Medical School/UCLA research team made a world map on the distribution and made some predictions about where Denisovan and Neanderthal genes may be impacting modern human biology.[[287]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-293)[[288]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-294)

For example, comparative studies in the mid-2010s found several [traits](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Phenotypic_trait "Phenotypic trait") related to neurological, immunological,[[289]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-295) developmental, and metabolic phenotypes, that were developed by archaic humans to European and Asian environments and inherited to modern humans through admixture with local hominins.[[290]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-296)[[291]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-297)

Although the narratives of human evolution are often contentious, several discoveries since 2010 show that human evolution should not be seen as a simple linear or branched progression, but a mix of related species.[[44]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-pmid21179161-46)[[5]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Denisovans_&_Neandertals-5)[[6]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Human_Hybrids-6)[[7]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Mosaic_humans,_the_hybrid_species-7) In fact, genomic research has shown that hybridization between substantially diverged lineages is the rule, not the exception, in human evolution.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Ackermann_2015-4) Furthermore, it is argued that hybridization was an essential creative force in the emergence of modern humans.[[4]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Ackermann_2015-4)

Stone tools are first attested around 2.6 million years ago, when hominins in Eastern Africa used so-called core [tools](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oldowan "Oldowan"), [choppers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chopper_(archaeology) "Chopper (archaeology)") made out of round cores that had been split by simple strikes.[[292]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Plummer-298) This marks the beginning of the [Paleolithic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paleolithic "Paleolithic"), or Old [Stone Age](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stone_Age "Stone Age"); its end is taken to be the end of the last [Ice Age](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Last_glacial_period "Last glacial period"), around 10,000 years ago. The Paleolithic is subdivided into the [Lower Paleolithic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lower_Paleolithic "Lower Paleolithic") (Early Stone Age), ending around 350,000–300,000 years ago, the [Middle Paleolithic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Middle_Paleolithic "Middle Paleolithic") (Middle Stone Age), until 50,000–30,000 years ago, and the [Upper Paleolithic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Upper_Paleolithic "Upper Paleolithic"), (Late Stone Age), 50,000–10,000 years ago.

Archaeologists working in the Great Rift Valley in Kenya have discovered the oldest known stone tools in the world. Dated to around 3.3 million years ago, the implements are some 700,000 years older than stone tools from Ethiopia that previously held this distinction.[[195]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Harmand_310%E2%80%93315-199)[[293]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-299)[[294]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-300)[[295]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-301)

The period from 700,000 to 300,000 years ago is also known as the [Acheulean](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Acheulean "Acheulean"), when _H.ergaster_ (or _erectus_) made large stone [hand axes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hand_axe "Hand axe") out of [flint](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Flint "Flint") and [quartzite](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Quartzite "Quartzite"), at first quite rough (Early Acheulian), later "[retouched](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Retouch_(lithics) "Retouch (lithics)")" by additional, more-subtle strikes at the sides of the [flakes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lithic_flake "Lithic flake"). After 350,000 BP the more refined so-called [Levallois technique](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Levallois_technique "Levallois technique") was developed, a series of consecutive strikes, by which scrapers, slicers ("racloirs"), needles, and flattened needles were made.[[292]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Plummer-298) Finally, after about 50,000 BP, ever more refined and specialized flint tools were made by the Neanderthals and the immigrant [Cro-Magnons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cro-Magnon "Cro-Magnon") (knives, blades, skimmers). Bone tools were also made by _H. sapiens_ in Africa by 90,000–70,000 years ago[[202]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-Henshilwood_etal_2002-206)[[296]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Human_evolution#cite_note-302) and are also known from early _H. sapiens_ sites in Eurasia by about 50,000 years ago.

This list is in [chronological](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chronological "Chronological") order across the table by _**[genus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Genus "Genus")**_. Some species/subspecies names are well-established, and some are less established – especially in genus _Homo_. Please see articles for more information.
